                  PREFACE

       Every law school in the country requires its students to take a first
semester first year course that teaches them how to write a research
memorandum and introduces them to the type of work that is done by a
young associate at a law firm. And all of those law schools also require
their students to take a second semester first year course that teaches them
how to write a trial brief (or an appeal brief) and introduces them to
litigation practice (or appellate practice). About half of the lawyers in the
U.S. are litigators. The other half are transactional attorneys. Yet there are
only a handful of law schools that require their students to take a third
course that teaches them how to draft a contract and introduces them to
transactional practice. That is the subject of this book.

       One way to describe what contract drafting and transactional practice
entails is to compare it to what is involved in the other types of writing
lawyers do. For example, when a young lawyer is assigned to write a
research memo or a trial memo (or appeal brief), they usually start by
obtaining the facts from an older associate or a partner in the firm, or a
document filed in the litigation. The facts describe an event or a series of
events that happened in the past. The lawyer's primary concern in
describing the facts in a research memo is to make sure the description is
accurate and complete. If the lawyer is writing an appeal brief, their
primary concern is to ensure the description of the facts is also persuasive.

       When a lawyer writes a research memo or a trial memo (or appeal
brief), the core of the document is the attorney's analysis of some legal
issue. In the document, the attorney states the facts, applies the law
(statutes and cases) to the facts, and comes to a conclusion. The facts are
important but the analysis is what the attorney devotes their primary
attention to.

       In addition, legal memos, trial memos and appeal briefs are usually
written from scratch (i.e. starting with a blank page). And legal writing is
usually done by one lawyer working alone. For example, in litigation, the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
lawyer representing one party writes a memo for their client, and the lawyer
for the other party writes a separate memo for their client.

       When a transactional attorney drafts a contract the situation is
entirely different. They usually obtain the facts (the terms of the deal)
directly from the client. Some of those facts may involve past events, but
most of them describe things that will happen in the future. Part of the
attorney's job is to translate that information into the language and
organization customarily used for legal agreements. But the attorney also
contributes to the substance by brainstorming for mechanisms to effectuate
the client's intent and provisions to protect the client in the event various
contingencies occur.

       When a lawyer drafts a contract, the core of the contract is the
description of facts (the deal terms). There are circumstances where the
attorney is also called upon to apply the law to certain contract provisions.
That aspect of contract drafting is important, but it is not the primary thing
an attorney is involved in when they draft a contract for a client.

       In addition, contracts are not typically drafted from scratch. The
lawyer usually starts with a precedent or sample document. And contracts
are not typically drafted by one person alone. Both lawyers, each
representing their own client, usually collaborate in drafting a contract with
terms both sides are agreeable to. So the process of drafting a contract is
very different than the process of writing a research memo, trial memo or
appeal brief.

       In one respect, however, legal writing and contract drafting are
similar. Both topics involve more than just writing well. Before a law
student learns to write a research memo they learn about the sources of law
in the American legal system, and they learn how to research issues within
those sources. Legal research is an ancillary skill that is usually taught in
connection with a first semester legal writing class.

       After a litigator learns to write a trial memo or an appeal brief, they
also learn how to represent a client in oral argument. A significant portion
of a second semester class on persuasive writing is usually devoted to
arguing a trial memo or an appeal brief at a hearing before the applicable

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
court. Oral argument is another example of an ancillary skill taught in a
writing class.

       Contract drafting is similar. After a contract is drafted it is usually
also necessary to get deal closed: oversee the signing of the documents,
arrange for the perfection of any security interests and handle the
disbursement of any proceeds of the transaction. Conducting a closing is an
example of an ancillary skill involved in contract drafting.

       As any text on legal writing, the coverage of this book on contract
drafting goes beyond writing well. In the same way that a first semester
legal writing class prepares students to work as an associate in a law firm
and a second semester class gives them the skills they need to practice
litigation, this book includes everything students need to know to "hit the
ground running" as a transactional attorney.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
             INTRODUCTION

       This book will teach you how to draft a contract from scratch and
using precedents; and it will also introduce you to each of the other skills
required for transactional practice. The book starts with a chapter on
getting up to speed and properly conducting a client intake because the
information obtained in that initial client meeting is what determines the
substance of the agreement. And the substance of the agreement is more
important than the form.

       The following chapters explain how to consistently draft obligations,
rights, prohibitions and descriptive statements that are clear and
unambiguous. Also, the organization of the various sections of an
agreement is discussed, with plenty of examples of the two most basic types
of agreements (an agreement that involves a due diligence period and a
closing, and a term agreement).

       Then the book covers "brainstorming." By thinking about how to
structure provisions so they best accomplish the client's intent, worrying
about how things could go wrong, and making sure the client is protected
should that occur, lawyers generate the additional substance they are
expected to contribute to an agreement.

       The next chapters explain how to draft with precedent documents
(forms and samples) because in practice lawyers spend most of their time
working with precedents. There is no need for an attorney in practice to
draft a promissory note or a mortgage from scratch every time they are
asked to handle a commercial loan. Rather, the job of a transactional
lawyer is usually to tailor a set of existing forms to the terms of each deal;
and there is more to that process than just filling in the blanks in an existing
document.

       The process of drafting a contract also doesn't end when the
document is completed. Contract language is almost always negotiated in
commercial deals. When the negotiations are completed, counsel is usually

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
required to get the documents signed. Sometimes that involves conducting
a formal closing, other times the lawyer need only confirm the authority of
the signer to sign for an entity. Often ethical issues arise in connection with
that process that need to analyzed and resolved. And sometimes computers
are used to draft the documents and record the signatures of the parties.
Each of those topics is also discussed in the final chapters of the book.

       As you read each chapter develop a checklist of your own that you
can refer to when you draft contracts from scratch, revise complex forms or
do any of the other things taught in this book. Drafting a contract is not like
writing a memorandum. The conventions for drafting contracts are far more
in number. You don't have to memorize them all but you do need to keep
track of them, and keep what you learn in a form you can refer back to
when you draft.

       You also need to collect samples of the contract provisions you study
in this book and the sample documents provided here. You learned in a
class on legal writing how different drafting a legal memorandum is than
writing an essay. The process of writing enforceable obligations is even
more different than the type of writing you learned in your first year of law
school. Again, you don't have to memorize what you learn here, but you
should keep copies of well drafted provisions and documents you
encounter. That is what transactional lawyers do in practice. You should
start getting in the habit of doing that now.

       More than anything else however you need to practice. A course on
contract drafting is not a substantive course; a contract drafting course is a
skills course. And you learn a legal skill the same way you learn any other
skill: by practicing. You need an introduction to the relevant conventions,
and you need to see examples of how those conventions are applied in
varying contexts. But ultimately you learn to draft contracts by drafting
contracts.

       Develop a checklist, collect samples of contract provisions, and
practice as much as you can. Do all that you will be ready for transactional
practice by the end of this book.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
1. GETTING UP TO SPEED
(PREPARING TO DRAFT)

T he first step in contract drafting is getting up to speed. Before you
          even meet with the client to find out what the agreement is, you
          must prepare. And that means becoming familiar with the client's
business and the nature of the goods or services involved, doing a little
research to find out what the applicable law is, taking a look at some sample
agreements to see what the issues are and what information you will need,
and then conferencing with the client and flushing out the terms of the deal.

A. Find Out What Type of Agreement You Will be Drafting

If a client tells you he wants to hire you to draft an agreement, or a senior
partner tells you they have a client who wants the firm to draft an
agreement, the first thing you need to do is find out what type of agreement
you will be drafting. Don't just put the time and date of that initial meeting
in your calendar and then show up unprepared. You need to get up to speed
before you do anything and, to do that, you need to know what type of
agreement you will be drafting. Is the client a bank that wants to retain the
firm to close a commercial loan? Or is the client a wealthy young person
who wants you to draft an antenuptual agreement before their upcoming
marriage. You can't prepare if you have no idea what type of contract you
will be drafting, so step one is to find out as much as you can before that
initial client meeting takes place. Don't try to conduct a client intake for a
business agreement on the fly. You need to set up a meeting, in person,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
over the phone or on line, and then you need to use the time before the
meeting to get ready.

B. Learn About the Client's Business and the Goods or Services
Involved

Suppose the client is hiring you to draft an agreement to construct a
building. Do you know anything about the construction business?
Probably not. Why would you? You're a lawyer not a general contractor.
But if you have a contractor for a client, you need to develop some
familiarity with the construction business before you represent them. You
need to have some idea what is involved in constructing a building before
you can draft a construction agreement.

Usually there is a set of "plans and specifications" that describe the
structure. The plans, which are actually called "drawings," show the
dimensions and location of each room; the specifications detail the
materials to be used and the identity of things like appliances and
furnishings. Then there is a budget itemizing how much everything will
cost, and a schedule for when things will be done. Construction is not done
by one person or entity; there is usually a "general contractor" who works
with various "subcontractors" to get the work done. The people who pour
the foundation are different than the people who do the framing. The
electricians do the wiring and the plumbers do the piping. HVAC (heating,
ventilation, and air conditioning) is handled by one group, and drywall is
handled by another. And the coordination and payment of all these parties
is critical to the timely completion of the project.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
If there are changes (and there usually are), there is a "change order"
procedure that has to be gone through. If subcontractors don't get paid,
there are "material men's liens" that have to be dealt with. And that process
is usually governed by a state statute. If there is financing, the lender will
constantly be verifying that the loan is "in balance" (i.e. there is enough
money left to complete the project). If there is insurance, the insurer will
also want to monitor construction progress, so that the amount of coverage
can be adjusted as the building is constructed. At a minimum, you have to
be familiar with all of these things before you meet with the client to learn
the deal. If you don't do that preparation, communication with the client
may be hampered. You may not be able to draft the contract well, and you
may also cause the client to lose confidence in you. For a lawyer, that is the
beginning of the end. You can't risk letting that happen.

Learn as much as you can about the client and the client's business before
you meet. If you are not familiar with the product or service that will be
involved in the agreement, take the time to get up to speed. Learn the lingo
so you can understand what the client is telling you. The client will be
expecting you to have that familiarity. If you can at least give the
impression that you know what you are doing, the client will have
confidence in you, and the process will be facilitated.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
C. Do Some Research and Make Sure You are Familiar with the
Applicable Law

Depending on the type of transaction you are working on there are a variety
of different laws that may apply. It would be impossible to list them all.
What you have to do is think about the type of transaction, the subject
matter involved, and the business the parties are in; and then do some
research to see what law applies. The client will expect you to be familiar
with the law. If you aren't, you risk starting the relationship on the wrong
foot.

For example, if you are planning to draft a contract for the sale of goods,
you need to take a look at Article 2 of the Uniform Commercial Code. Do
that and you will see the requirements for excluding implied warranties in §
2-316, limiting consequential damages in § 2-719, providing for liquidated
damages in § 2-718, and shortening the statute of limitations in a sales
agreement, § 2-725. Those are all things you may want to add to an
agreement if you are drafting it on behalf of a seller. If you do want to add
one or more of those provisions, you will have to check the statute to see
what requirements there are for how the provision must be drafted.

If what your client is buying or selling is real estate, there are likely state
laws applicable to the transaction. For example, Florida sellers of real
estate are required to disclose the potential effect of the sale on ad valorem
taxes, § 689.261, Fla. Stat., the dangers associated with radon gas, §
404.056(5), Fla. Stat., the potential for erosion of coastal properties, §
161.57, Fla. Stat., and information about homeowner's associations and
condo associations, §§ 720.401 & 718.503, Fla. Stat. If you are drafting a
purchase and sale agreement on behalf of a seller, you may want to include
those disclosures in the agreement, so it is clear the applicable laws have
been complied with.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Article 2A of the Uniform Commercial Code applies to the lease of goods.
The statute contains many of the same provisions that are included in
Article 2, plus some additional sections only applicable to leases. For
example, limitations on choice of law and choice of forum provisions are
contained in § 2A-106. Choice of law and choice of forum provisions are
typically included in a commercial lease, so you will need to know what
those limitations are.

If the lease applies to residential real estate, then, again, there are likely a
series of state statutes that will apply to the agreement. For example,
Florida law requires a variety of disclosures be made and procedures be
followed whenever money is deposited or advanced by a tenant in
connection with a rental agreement, § 83.49, Fla. Stat. If the lease you are
drafting includes a security deposit, it would "behoove" you (as my old boss
used to say), to make sure whatever provisions you are including comply
with the statute.

Mortgages conveying a security interest in real property are also governed
by state statutes. In some states, mortgages actually convey title to the
lender ("title theory" states); in others mortgages convey a security interest
("lien theory" states). I think every state requires a mortgage to be recorded
to have priority over other subsequent creditors, but the place of recording
differs from state to state. Also, in some state non-judicial foreclosures can
be conducted under a "power of sale;" in other states, judicial approval of
the foreclosure process is required. So before you start drafting (or looking
for sample documents you might use), you will need to know what the
applicable law is.

State laws also regulate various types of consumer transactions and service
providers. For example, in Florida, there is a state statute regulating the
form and substance of membership campground agreements. §§ 509.501-
509.512, Fla. Stat. And there is another statute governing talent agencies
and the contracts they enter into. §§ 468.401-468.415, Fla. Stat. If you
draft a membership campground agreement or a talent agency contract in

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Florida without first researching the applicable statute, you risk producing a
document that violates state law.

Those are just a few examples. After you have familiarized yourself with
the client and the client's business, do some research to familiarize yourself
with the applicable law. You need that information to understand the
client's concerns and fulfill your role as counselor and draftsperson.

D. Review Sample Documents and Create a List of Questions

The next step is to take a look at some sample documents. One way to do
that is to do an internet search for the type of agreement you are interested
in. The problem is, if you do it that way, you are likely to find links to
websites selling agreement forms to consumers, like Rocket Lawyer and
Legal Zoom. But you don't want those types of documents. You want to
look at sample commercial agreements used by lawyers. So you need to go
to a law library (assuming libraries still exist after you graduate and start
practicing) or go to a website for attorneys. Westlaw has a service called
"Practical Law" that is a good source for sample agreements. The
Securities and Exchange Commission service called EDGAR (Electronic
Data Gathering, Analysis, and Retrieval) is free a source that can be used to
search for sample business documents. Also, there are free websites like
onecle.com and lawinsider.com that are additional sources for sample
agreements and contract provisions.

Note however the purpose of gathering sample documents at this stage is
not to find a form you can use. Rather it is to find out what issues are
typically dealt with in the type of agreement you will be drafting and what
pieces of information will be needed to complete the document. For
example, if you are drafting a commercial lease and you look at a typical
form, you will see that you will need the name and addresses of the parties,
the amount and payment terms for the rent, a detailed description of the
premises, and the amount and terms for any advance rent or security
deposits. Knowing that you can begin to make a list of questions you will

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
need to ask the client. And making that list will ensure the initial client
meeting is efficient and productive.

So, in addition to learning the clients business and becoming familiar with
the applicable law, also take the time to search for some sample documents
and develop a list of questions and issues for your meeting with the client.

E. Meet with the Client and Flush out the Terms of the Deal

The last step is to meet with the client and find out the terms of the
agreement. And the first thing to know about that step is that most people
don't like meeting with lawyers. I know it's hard to imagine, but for the
average person, going to see a lawyer is like going to the dentist. You know
you have to do it, but you don't want to, and you hope to get it over with as
soon as possible. For lawyers that means it isn't always easy to get the
client to sit down and go through all the facts of the deal with you. So,
make the process as painless as possible. Explain why you need to go
through this process and then do it efficiently. Have a list of questions or an
outline ready. Ask open ended questions, like you would in a deposition
(i.e. questions that begin with who, what, where, when, why, and how).
Listen carefully and take good notes.

If you are a litigator meeting with a client for the first time, you want to ask
open ended questions, listen carefully to the answers, and learn as much as
you can about what happened. But you also want the client to know you
empathize with their position. That is critical to establish in the first client
meeting. Similarly, if you are a transactional attorney doing an initial client
intake, you ask open ended questions, listen carefully to the answers and
learn as much as you can about the transaction you will be documenting.
But you also want to demonstrate to the client that you fully understand the
terms of the deal. That is what is critical to establish when you are hired to
draft a contract.

What you want is to get to the point where you know everything the client
knows. Who agreed to what? What did the other guy agree to in

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
exchange? What else was agreed upon? What representations were made?
You may be surprised to find there are a lot of things that weren't discussed
but should have been. Keep track of what those things were. And keep
asking questions until you have completely flushed out all the elements of
the deal, and all the details of each element.
There will come a time when you actually know the deal better than the
client. But for now, just make sure you know everything the client knows.
All the information in the client's head has to be transferred to your head for
you to transcribe that information in a contract. Plus you need to
understand the client's interests and concerns. You need to understand what
is important to them and what they are looking for your to contribute. Also,
try to establish a rapport with the client; and leave the door open when you
are done. After the next step you will need to talk to them again.
Those are the four steps to getting up to speed. Make yourself familiar with
the goods or services involved. Make sure you are on top of the applicable
law. Review some sample agreements and make an outline of questions to
ask. And then meet with the client to flush out the terms of the deal. Once
you have done those four things, you should have everything you need to
start drafting.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
                                 Practice Exercises

1. A client is retaining us to draft a purchase agreement for beer
brewing equipment. When I talked to her on the phone she said the
agreement should be contingent on the inspection of the "mash tun" and
the "hot liquor tank." Also, the seller has a "CIP" system and should be
required to use it to clean out the "wort" before the inspection happens. I
am not familiar with that terminology. Please do some quick research on
line and send me an e-mail memo explaining what she meant so I can
prepare for the initial client conference.

2. A firm client has hired is to draft a promissory note for a finance
transaction. When I talked to him on the phone he said this would be a
little different than a conventional lending arrangement. The loan would
have a 20 year term but a 30 year "amortization." The lender isn't
worried about the structure because rates are going down and there should
be no problem doing a "refi" when the "balloon" is due. I am not familiar
with these terms. Please do some quick research on line and send me an
e-mail memo explaining what he meant so I can get up to speed before I
discuss the details of the loan with the client.

3. A client contacts you to draft a commercial lease for office space.
She says the lease should be "triple net." The rent is $1,500 per month
plus "CAM" charges. The landlord will provide everything up to the
"rough plumbing" and the tenant will do responsible for any build out
from there. Likely you are familiar with a lease agreement, having signed
one for the apartment you live in now. If you are not familiar with this
terminology, please do some quick research on line and send me an e-mail
memo explaining what she meant so you will be prepared for an initial
meeting with the client to discuss deal terms.

4. Your next project will be to draft a residential lease for property in
the state you live in. To prepare, you need to do some legal research on
the statutory requirements for a residential lease. The landlord will be
collecting the last month's rent, as well as a security deposit, as part of the
deal. Are there any disclosures or notices that have to be included in the
agreement? Are there any restrictions on provisions that may or may not
be included?

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
5. You need to prepare for a client conference for a real estate
purchase. Go on line and collect some real estate purchase and sale
agreements. Make sure the samples were drafted for a small real estate
transaction in the state in which you live. Also, try to find forms drafted
for a seller, not a buyer. Then read through the forms to find out the
information you will need to produce a final draft; and outline that
information to create a list of questions for a client conference.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
2. DRAFTING OBLIGATIONS,
RIGHTS, PROHIBITIONS AND
DESCRIPTIVE STATEMENTS

C ontracts typically contain four types of provisions: obligations,
           rights, prohibitions, and descriptive statements. To draft contract
           provisions clearly you have to first be able to determine what type
of provision is involved. You have to take what the client tells you and
figure out if what they are describing is an obligation, prohibition, right or
descriptive statement.

Obligations are provisions that require a party to do something in the future
they are not already required to do. If the client tells you they have a buyer
who has agreed to buy property they are selling, and the client wants you to
memorialize that agreement in a written contract, the provision you need to
draft is an example of an obligation. The buyer will not be legally obligated
to follow through with the purchase until you create that obligation in the
contract, and the buyer agrees to it by signing on the bottom line.

Rights are provisions that enable a party to do something in the future they
don't already have the ability to do. If the client also tells you they have
agreed that the buyer can inspect the property before the sale, the provision
you need to draft is an example of a right. A buyer doesn't normally have
the ability to inspect something they don't own. To enable them to do that
you need to draft a contract provision giving them the legal right to do the
inspection.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Prohibitions prohibit a party from doing something they would otherwise be
able to do. The client may have further agreed not to market the property
once the agreement is signed. That is an example of a prohibition. A seller
normally has the ability to market property they are offering for sale. To
prevent a seller from doing that, you need to draft a prohibition.

Obligations, rights and prohibitions concern things that happen in the
future. By drafting those provisions in a contract you create enforceable
rules for the future relations of the parties.

Descriptive statements are the fourth type of contract provision. Unlike
obligations, rights and prohibitions, descriptive statements usually refer to
things that happened in the past or are happening in the present (i.e. when
the contract is signed). The background section of a contract (sometimes
called the recitals) is comprised of descriptive statements. If the client tells
you the property is 2,000 square feet, and you want to add that information
to the contract, what you need to draft is an example of a descriptive
statement. Unlike obligations, rights and prohibitions, descriptive
statements usually do not create enforceable rules.

The cardinal rule for the court's enforcement of obligations, rights and
prohibitions according to their plain meaning and intent is that the language
of the provision must be "clear and unambiguous." E.g., M & G Polymers
USA, LLC v. Tackett, 135 S. Ct. 926, 190 L. Ed. 2d 809 (2015) (If the words
of a contract are clear and unambiguous, their meaning is to be ascertained
in accordance with the contract's plainly expressed intent), Lyme Land
Conservation Trust, Inc. v. Platner, 325 Conn. 737, 159 A.3d 666
(2017) (When the intent of the parties is clear and unambiguous, contractual
terms are to be given their ordinary meaning and there is no room for
construction), Bathla v. 913 Market, LLC, 200 A.3d 754 (Del. 2018)(Clear
and unambiguous contractual terms will be interpreted according to their
ordinary meaning), Sky Canyon Properties, LLC v. Golf Club at Black Rock,
LLC, 155 Idaho 604, 315 P.3d 792 (2013) (If the contract is clear and
unambiguous, the contract's meaning and legal effect is to be determined
from the plain meaning of its words), Haegert v. University of Evansville,
977 N.E.2d 924, 286 Ed. Law Rep. 660 (Ind. 2012) (If the contract terms

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
are drafted in clear and unambiguous language, the court will enforce the
contract according to those terms), Littleton Const. Ltd. v. Huber Const.,
Inc., 27 N.Y.3d 1081, 35 N.Y.S.3d 681, 55 N.E.3d 441 (2016) (If the
contract is clear and unambiguous, it must be enforced according to its plain
meaning), Hexagon Holdings, Inc. v. Carlisle Syntec Incorporated, 199
A.3d 1034 (R.I. 2019) (When the language of the contract is clear and
unambiguous, the words will be given their usual and ordinary meaning),
Harleysville Group Insurance v. Heritage Communities, Inc., 420 S.C. 321,
803 S.E.2d 288 (2017) (The language alone determines the contract's force
and effect, as long as it is clear and unambiguous), Parsons v. Associated
Banc-Corp, 2017 WI 37, 374 Wis. 2d 513, 893 N.W.2d 212 (2017) (The
court construes the contract according to its literal terms, as long as the
terms are clear and unambiguous), Pennaco Energy, Inc. v. Sorenson, 2016
WY 34, 371 P.3d 120 (Wyo. 2016) (When the language of a contract is
clear and unambiguous, a court's inquiry is limited to the four corners of the
document).

Clarity is typically achieved in three ways. First, use simple active voice
sentences (i.e. subject, verb, object). Start with the one of the parties as the
subject of the sentence and just say what it is you are obligating them to do,
giving them the right to do or prohibiting them from doing.

Second, be consistent with the language you use to describe obligations,
rights and prohibitions. In practice you will see lawyers using the words
"agrees," "promises," "shall," "must," and "will" for obligations.
Regardless of which word they use, they will use it consistently.

Think of the Ten Commandments in the Bible. When Moses came down
from the mountain, he didn't say "Thou shalt not kill." "Thou may not
commit adultery." "Thou must not steal." "Thou will not bear false witness
against thy neighbor." "Coveting thy neighbor's goods shall not be done
either." He didn't mix it up. He used the phrase "shalt not" over and over
again. When you draft obligations, rights and prohibitions, you should do
the same thing. You should be consistent with the language you use.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Believe it or not, there is much scholarly debate in the literature about
whether "shall" or "will" is the "correct" word to use for obligations. E.g.,
Chadwick C. Busk, Using Shall or Will to Create Obligations in Business
Contracts, Mich. Bar J. (2017). Some of the leading scholars in this area
argue "shall" is correct, "will" is wrong. E.g., Kenneth A. Adams, A
Manual of Style for Contract Drafting (4th ed. 2017), Tina L. Stark,
Drafting Contracts: How and Why Lawyers Do What They Do (2d ed.
2007). Others argue "will" is correct, "shall" is wrong. Brian A. Garner,
Guidelines for Drafting and Editing Contracts (1st ed. 2019). My position
is either is fine as long as you are consistent. I personally think "shall"
sounds old fashioned. It's not a word I would otherwise use in my writing,
so I don't use it in contracts either. In this book, I use "will" for obligations,
"will not" for prohibitions and "may" for rights.
Third, make sure your provisions are complete. To do that, ask what a
colleague of mine used to refer to as the "w" questions: "who," "what," "to
whom," "when," "where" and "how" ("how" ends with a "w" so that's a
"w" question too). Not all of those questions will apply to every obligation,
right or prohibition you draft but you should have answers to as many of
them as do apply.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Be consistent with your descriptive statements too. If you write in the
agreement the contractor will provide their own "tools, materials, and
supplies," then you should use that phrase and only that phrase every time
you describe what the contractor is required to provide.

Also, when you transcribe information the client gave you, make sure you
are specific and your transcription is accurate. If the client gives you a
detailed description of the subject matter of an agreement, your description
in the contract should be at least as detailed, and it should be accurate. It is
usually not appropriate to paraphrase or summarize in a contract. Your
document should typically be at least as detailed as the information you get
from the client.

Those are the basic principles you need to follow when drafting obligations,
rights, prohibitions and descriptions. Here are some more specific
examples of how to draft each of those types of provisions.

A. Draft Obligations Using "Will"

A tenant's agreement to pay rent to a landlord is an example of a
obligation. To make it clear an obligation is intended, start with the name
of the party you are assigning the obligation to, use a word like "will" or
"shall" as a helping verb (Again, I will use the word "will"), and just state
what the person agreed to do, like this:

 The Tenant will pay the rent.

To make the provision complete, ask yourself to whom will the rent be
paid? How much is the rent? When is the rent due? And how is the rent to
be paid? Then add the answers to those questions to your original
provision, like this:

 On or before the first day of each month, the Tenant will pay rent to
 the Landlord in the amount of five hundred dollars ($500) by check

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 or money order.

Assuming the rent actually is $500 per month, you have now drafted a clear,
complete, and accurate obligation requiring the tenant to pay the rent.

One thing to avoid when drafting obligations is obligating the parties to
come to an agreement in the future, like this:

 The Landlord and the Tenant will agree on an appropriate late charge.

An agreement to agree is generally not enforceable. Plus, the whole idea of
drafting a contract is to help the parties to come to an agreement now, and
then put that agreement in writing. So don't draft provisions indicating the
parties "will agree" to things after the contract is signed.

B. Draft Rights Using "May"

A landlord's right to conduct periodic inspections of the property is an
example of a contract right. To draft a right, start with the party's name, use
the word "may" as a helping verb, and then state what the person "may"
do. Don't mix it up and use "may" for some rights and "can" or "has the
right to" for others. Be consistent. Also, make sure each provision is
complete by asking myself "who," "what," "to whom," "when," and "how,"
as applicable.

For example, to draft a right to inspect an apartment, start with a simple
active voice sentence, using the word "may" before the verb, like this:

 The Landlord may inspect the Unit.

Then ask yourself when the inspection will occur and how it must be done?
Is notice required? To whom will it be given? And when will it be given?

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
And add the answers to those questions to what you initially drafted, like
this:

 The Landlord may inspect the Unit on any weekday between the
 hours of 9:00 a.m. and 5:00 p.m. The Landlord will give notice to the
 Tenant at least twenty-four hours before the proposed inspection.

That is an example of a clear, complete and accurate provision granting a
landlord the right to inspect a unit, and obligating the landlord to give
notice before doing so.

If the provision gets lengthy, you can break it up, as I did in that example.
Every provision in a contract does not have to be a single sentence. But if
you do break it up, continue to follow the same conventions. Don't say
"Notice will be given to the Tenant...." Obligate someone to give the notice,
even if it is implicit who that party is. Say instead "The Landlord will give
notice to the Tenant...."

Also, there are a couple things to avoid when using the word "may." The
first is to avoid giving someone the right to do something they already have
the right to do, like this:

 The Tenant may request that the term of the Lease be extended.

The Tenant can request whatever the Tenant wants to request. It is not
necessary to give the Tenant the right to do something he already has the
right to do.

The second is to avoid characterizing an obligation as a right. If you want
the Tenant to return the keys to the property, do it like this:

 The Tenant will return the keys to the Property to the Landlord.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Not like this:

 The Landlord may receive the keys to the Property from the Tenant.

Among other things, if the Tenant doesn't return the keys, you want to be
able to enforce a provision of the Lease obligating the Tenant to return
them. A Lease provision saying the Landlord has the right to receive the
keys will be of little use to you.

C. Draft Prohibitions Using "Will Not"

A provision taking away the tenant's right to have pets in the unit is an
example of a prohibition. Since I am using the word "will" for obligations,
I will use the phrase "will not" for prohibitions. I will not use any other
phrase like "may not" or "cannot."

To draft a prohibition, start with the same simple active voice sentence
structure you have been using for obligations and rights:

 The Tenant will not have pets.

The only "w" questions that apply here are where and when. So, finish by
adding the answers to those questions, like this:

 The Tenant will not have pets in the Unit at any time during the
 Term.

And you are done. Easy peasy.
The primary thing to avoid when using "will not" is using that phrase for
something that should be a descriptive statement, not a prohibition. If the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
agreement is for the tenant to have free access to the exercise room, don't
draft the applicable provision like this:

 The Tenant will not pay for access to the exercise room.

You don't need to prohibit the tenant from paying for that access. You just
need to make clear they don't have to pay for that access. Saying a party
doesn't have the obligation to do something is not the same as saying the
party is prohibited from doing it. The former is a descriptive statement; the
latter is a prohibition. What you want here is a descriptive statement, like
this:

 The Tenant's access to the exercise room is provided free of charge.

D. Draft Descriptions Using Past or Present Tense Statements

The other type of provision you will find in contracts is a descriptive
statement. Descriptive statements are past or present tense statements
describing factual information in an agreement. Examples of descriptive
statements include the provisions of the background section and
representations and warranties.

To draft a descriptive statement, use the same simple active voice sentence
structure you would use for other contract provisions. Make sure the
factual information you include is accurate and complete. And even though
there is no one word or phrase you can use to signal a descriptive statement,
still be consistent with the wording you do use.

For example, to describe a landlord's business in the background section of
a lease, simply state what that business is, like this:

 The Landlord owns and manages over 1,000 rental units in northern
 central Florida.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Also, since you initially refer to the rental properties as "units," don't refer
to them elsewhere in the agreement as "apartments." Consistently use the
word "units" every time you refer to them in the agreement.

Another example of a descriptive statement is a representation and
warranty. If you want to include that type of statement in a contract, it is
important to state who is making the statement and to whom it is being
made, like this:

 The Borrower represents and warrants to the Lender that it is a duly
 organized and existing New York corporation.

Representations and warranties actually serve two functions. One is to
create a default if it turns out the information isn't true. If the borrower in
the above example isn't duly organized and existing, and the lender finds
out, the borrower will be in default, and the lender will be able to call the
loan. The other is sometimes called an "audit function." When a party asks
a corporation to represent that it is duly organized and existing, what the
party really want is for the corporation's lawyer to check and make sure the
corporation has been following all the required corporate formalities. In
other words, you add the representation so the other party will check and
make sure it's true before the document is signed.

The differences between a representation and warranty and any other
descriptive statement are the following: (1) a representation and warranty is
a statement that is relied on by the other party to enter into the agreement;
and (2) the other party has the right to end the relationship or otherwise
back out of the deal if it turns out the representation and warranty is not
true. So, for example, if one party is paying substantially more money for
a small house because they were told the house was the boyhood home of
former president George Washington, and, if it turns out that is not true, the
party does not want to go through with the purchase, then that statement
should be a representation and warranty.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
When you are drafting descriptive statements beware of future tense
statements. Often a future tense descriptive statement is more appropriately
drafted as an obligation or a past or present tense statement. For example, a
provision stating "the Equipment will be clean and ready for use by the
Closing" is likely inappropriate. If you want to make sure Equipment is
clean, you need to obligate a party to the agreement to clean it. A
descriptive statement won't accomplish that result. Similarly, a provision
stating "the Equipment will include batteries" is also likely inappropriate. If
the Equipment includes batteries then make that part of the definition for
the term Equipment. Alternatively, if you haven't defined the term
Equipment include the batteries in your description of what the equipment
is.

The hard part for a novice draftsperson is not so much drafting the
provision correctly, it is recognizing when something the client tells you is
an obligation, when it is a right, when it is a prohibition, and when it is a
descriptive statement. Whenever one party wants to obligate another party
to do something in the future, whether they use the words "agrees to," or "is
responsible for," or "promises," what they are describing is an obligation.
If I were drafting the applicable provision, I would use the word "will" in
that situation.

Whenever one party gives another party the right to do something in the
future, whether they use the words "can" or "has discretion to," that is an
example of a right. In that situation, I would use the word "may" to draft
the clause.

And when one party wants to prohibit another party from doing something,
whether they use the words "can't," "may not," or "prohibit," they are
describing a prohibition. If you are using "shall" for obligations, use "shall
not" for prohibitions. As for me, I prefer to use "will not." What is
important is not so much which word you choose, but that you use it
consistently. To draft clearly and without ambiguity, you must distinguish

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
among obligations, rights, prohibitions and descriptive statements, and draft
each type of provision the same way every time.

                                  Practice Exercises
             In the Appendix to this book there are instructions (either a
 memorandum from a senior attorney or a transcript of an initial client
 intake) for drafting the following contracts:
         1. Associate Employment Agreement
         2. Laptop Purchase Agreement
         3. Residential Lease Agreement
         4. Sale of Assets Agreement and
         5. Consulting Services Agreement.
         Before drafting one or more of those agreements, take each piece
 of information in the instructions and determine whether it should

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
properly be drafted as an obligation, a right, a prohibition or a descriptive
statement. To complete this exercise, use the following chart:

Information   Obligation?  Right  Prohibition?   Descriptive
from          Draft using  ?      Draft using    Statement?
Instructions  "will" or    Draft  "will not" or  Draft as a
              "shall"      using  "shall not"    Past or
                           "may"                 Present Tense
                                                 Statement

              Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
3. AVOIDING POTENTIALLY
AMBIGUOUS WORDS AND
PHRASES

I n addition to clearly, consistently and completely drafting obligations,
      rights, prohibitions, and descriptive statements, there are a number of
      potentially ambiguous words and phrases you need to avoid. Using
"months" or the word "within" in deadlines can make them unclear.
Misplacing time periods and dates in conditions can create ambiguity; and
making an indefinite pronoun reference also makes a sentence unclear. It is
important to avoid the mistakes of leaving the party out of obligations and
attempting to obligate a non-party. Don't use phrases like and/or and etc. in
an agreement. And avoid excessive legalese and redundancy in the contract
provisions you draft.

A. Make Clear the Trigger Date, Duration, and End Date for Time
Deadlines

Time deadlines are primary source of ambiguity in contracts. You may not
have thought about it, but a statement like "the Contractor will pay the
Subcontractor within one (1) month" is rife with ambiguity. One month
from when? The statement doesn't say when the clock starts ticking.
Which month? Not all months are 30 days long. February has twenty-eight
days; January has thirty-one days. Is one month from February 15th, March
15th (the same day a month later) or March 17th (30 days later)? Also, can

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
the Subcontractor pay on the last day or do they have to pay before the last
day? Does "within" mean "on or before" or "before"? There is no clear
answer to any of those questions.

To avoid these ambiguities, there are three things you need to address when
you draft a time deadline. First, use language that clearly indicates whether
payment can happen on the last day or if it must be received before the last
day, like "on or before," "before," "on or after," "after," "no later than," or
"at least." Don't use words like "within" or "by." So, you would start like
this:

 The Contractor will pay the Subcontractor on or before

Rather than use months to describe the time period (or even weeks), it is
preferable to use days. Why? Because every day is twenty-four hours long,
no matter which day it is. So, draft it like this:

 The Contractor will pay the Subcontractor on or before the thirtieth
 (30th) day

And don't forget to include the date the period starts to run. Some
practitioners refer to that as the "trigger date."

 The Contractor will pay the Subcontractor on or before the thirtieth
 (30th) day after the Project is completed.

Also, ask yourself what should happen if the last day of the time period is a
Saturday or Sunday or a legal holiday. Likely, it is not the parties intent to
require performance on a weekend or a holiday. The way to remedy that is
to add an interpretive statement, like this:

 "Business Day" means Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday or
 Friday, but does not include any federal holiday. If any deadline or

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 time period in this Agreement ends on a day other than a Business
 Day, then performance is due on the next Business Day.
Now that is much clearer than "The Contractor will pay the Subcontractor
within one (1) month."

B. Don't "Sandwich" the Time Period in Conditions
Conditions are another potential source of ambiguity in agreements.
Drafting them is no different than the way you would do it normally (use
"if" and "then," "unless" or "except"). An obligation can be subject to a
condition, a grant of rights can be subject to a condition, and a prohibition
can be subject to a condition. For example, failing to pay timely is usually
the condition to incurring a late fee:
 If the Contractor fails to timely pay the Subcontractor, then the
 Contractor will pay the Subcontractor a late fee in the amount of
 $25.00.
When you draft a provision like this be careful not to "sandwich" a time
period in the middle of the condition. If you do that your condition will be
ambiguous. Here is an example:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 If the Contractor fails to timely pay the Subcontractor on or before the
 thirtieth (30th) day after the Project is completed the Contractor will pay
 the Subcontractor a late fee in the amount of $25.00.

Does the Contractor have to pay the Subcontractor on or before the thirtieth
day after the Project is completed? Or does the Contractor have to pay the
late fee on or before the thirtieth day after the Project is completed? The
provision is ambiguous because the time deadline is "sandwiched" right in
between the two phrases. It could apply to the first phrase or it could apply
to the second phrase. To resolve this ambiguity, make sure you put the time
deadline within the phrase to which it applies, like this:

 If the Contractor fails to timely pay the Subcontractor, the
 Contractor will pay the Subcontractor a late fee in the amount of
 $25.00 on or before the thirtieth (30th) day after the Project is
 completed .

This principle applies to modifiers generally. A modifier is a word or
phrase in a sentence that modifies another word or phrase. If you misplace
a modifier, the result is an ambiguous sentence. To rectify that issue,
remember to always place the modifier as close as possible to the word or
phrase it modifies.

C. Avoid Gender Pronouns

I would also recommend that you avoid gender specific pronouns like "he,"
"she," "his," and "hers" in your contracts. If you use them in a provision in
a form document, like this: "the Broker will perform his duties to the
satisfaction of the Seller," you are going to have to remember to change
"his" to "her" every time the gender of the broker changes. Or you could
have one set of forms for men and another set for women. But the preferred
method would be to get into the habit of not using the pronouns at all, like
this: "the Broker will perform the Broker's duties to the satisfaction of the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Seller." Do that and you won't have to worry about checking all your
pronouns every time you draft a document.

Another reason to omit pronouns is because they can be a source of
ambiguity if not used properly. If you write a sentence like this: "The
Contractor will arrange for delivery of the Equipment to the Subcontractor,
at his sole cost and expense" it is unclear whether "his" refers to the
Contractor or the Subcontractor. That is called an indefinite pronoun
reference; and it also makes a sentence ambiguous. To fix it, omit the
pronoun, like this: "The Contractor will arrange for the delivery of the
Equipment to the Subcontractor, at the Contractor's sole cost and expense."

You should also note that using the words "they" or "their" as a substitute
for gender pronouns does not solve the potential ambiguity in the previous
sentence. If you had written "The Contractor will arrange for delivery of
the Equipment to the Subcontractor, at their sole cost and expense" the
sentence would have still been ambiguous. So I would also recommend
you avoid gender neutral pronouns, unless it is clear who the pronoun refers
to.

D. Don't Omit the Party from Obligations, Rights and Prohibitions

Make sure you don't leave the party out of your obligations, rights and
prohibitions. In other words, don't write: "The house will have a concrete
foundation." If you are drafting an obligation, you need to say who you are
obligating to do the work. If you don't, you have created what's called a
"false obligation." The obligation is there but it isn't given to anyone. To
rectify that issue, you would instead say "the Builder will construct the
house with a concrete foundation." Again, if you are drafting an obligation,
right, or prohibition, start with the name of the party you are obligating,
empowering, or prohibiting. And then, you won't have to worry about
creating false obligations.

E. Don't Obligate, Prohibit, or Empower People Who Are Not Parties to
the Contract

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Also make sure you don't include non-parties in your obligations, rights,
and prohibitions. For example, if your contract is between a Contractor and
a Subcontractor, don't draft an obligation that purports to obligate
employees of the Subcontractor to do something. The employees are not
parties to the contract. Rather, obligate the Subcontractor to instruct its
employees to do something or obligate the Subcontractor to ensure that they
do it. So, don't write "Employees of the Subcontractor will obtain adequate
training before operating the Equipment." Write something like "the
Subcontractor will adequately train the Subcontractor's employees before
permitting them to use the Equipment."

F. Avoid Using "And/Or" and "Etc."

The phrase "and/or" is yet another source of ambiguity. The word "or"
means one, the other (either), or both, so just write "or", not "and/or". In a
famous Florida case the court admonished against the use of "and/or",
stating:

    ...we take our position with that distinguished company of lawyers
    who have condemned its use. It is one of those inexcusable
    barbarisms which were sired by indolence and damned by
    indifference, and has no more place in legal terminology than the
    vernacular of Uncle Remus has in Holy Writ. I am unable to divine
    how such senseless jargon becomes current. The coiner of it
    certainly had no appreciation for terse and concise law English.

Cochrane v. Fla. E. Coast Rwy. Co., 145 So. 217, 218-219 (Fla. 1932).
Instead of writing "The Tenant may use the patio and/or the lanai attached
to the Unit," revise the language to read "The Tenant may use the patio
attached to the Unit or the lanai attached to the Unit" or, if it is appropriate
to make crystal clear the use of both is also permitted, then write "The
Tenant may use the patio attached to the Unit or the lanai attached to the
Unit or both."

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
It should go without saying that the abbreviation "etc." is not appropriate to
use in a written contract; and writing it out as "et cetera" is not appropriate
either. The abbreviation and the word mean "the rest." If you want to
include "the rest" in a contact, you need to say what the rest is. Instead of
writing, "the Unit is comprised of one bedroom, one bathroom, etc." say
"the Unit is comprised of one bedroom, one bathroom, one living room, a
galley kitchen, a covered patio and a single parking space."

G. Avoid Excessive Legalese

Avoid using excessive legalese in a modern business agreement. We don't
wear powdered wigs anymore. We don't go to work on horseback. And no
one still has wooden teeth. The 1800's have come and gone. We should
use the language of the age in which we practice. We shouldn't use phrases
like "witnesseth," "whereas," "hereinafter," "now therefore," or
"hereinbefore" in contracts because no one talks that way anymore.

I know many lawyers would argue that adding just a little legalese here and
there doesn't hurt anything and it makes the document look more
"official." In other words, it's a matter of style. So, to be clear, if you are
revising someone else's document that has legalese in it, I would not
recommend that you go through the document and delete all the archaic
expressions. But if you are drafting from scratch, my personal preference is
to avoid using legalese altogether.

However, I also recognize the real problem is with overusing those types of
archaic legal expressions. Consider the following example of an indentured
servant agreement from the 1700's. This contract is very simple and short,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
but the legalese is so intense it is hard to decipher the intent. If you want to
understand why educators rail against legalese, try reading this document:

    Indentured Servant Contract

    This Indenture, Made the Fourth Day of August in the Twenty-ninth
    Year of the Reign of our Sovereign Lord George the Second King of
    Great Britain, And in the Year of our Lord, One Thousand Seven
    Hundred and fifty five Between William Buckland of Baford
    Carpenter & Joiner of the one Part, and Thomson Mason of
    London, Esq. of the other Part, Witnesseth, That the said William
    Buckland for the Consideration herein after-mentioned, hath, and
    by these Presents doth Covenant, Grant, and Agree to, and with the
    said Thomas Mason Executors and assigns, That He the said
    William Buckland shall and will, as a faithful Covenant Servant,
    well and truly serve the said Thomas Mason his Executors and
    assigns in the Plantation of Virginia beyond the Seas, for the Space
    of Four Years, next ensuing his Arrival in the said Plantation in the
    Employment of a Carpenter and Joiner. And the said William
    Buckland doth hereby Covenant and Declare himself, now to be the
    Age of Twenty two Years Single and no Covenant or contracted
    Servant to any other Person or Persons, And the said Thomas
    Mason for himself his Executors Or Assigns, in Consideration
    thereof, doth hereby Covenant, Promise And Agree to and with the
    said William Buckland his Executors, and Assigns, that He the said
    Thomas Mason his Executors or Assigns, shall and will at his or
    their own proper Costs and Charges, with what convenient Speed
    they may, carry and convey, or cause to be carried and conveyed
    over unto the said Plantation, the said William Buckland and from
    henceforth, and during the said Voyage, and also during the said
    Term, shall and will at the like Costs and Charges, provide for and
    allow the said William Buckland all necessary Meat, Drink,
    Washing, and Lodging, fit and convenient for William as Covenant
    Servants in such Cases are usually provided for and allowed and

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
    pay and allow William Buckland Wages on Salary at the Rate of
    Twenty Pounds Sterling per Annum Payable Quarterly And for the
    true Performance of the Premises, the said Parties, the these
    Presents bind themselves, their Executors and Administrators, the
    either to the other, in the Penal Sum of Forty Pounds Sterling,
    firmly by these Presents. In witness whereof, they have hereunto
    interchanged by set their Hands and Seals, the Day and Year above-
    written.

Here is the same agreement without the legalese and applying the protocols
I discussed in Chapters Two and Three:

    Indentured Servant Contract

    On August 4, 1755, William Buckland (Buckland) and Thomson
    Mason (Mason) agree as follows:

    1. Buckland will serve Mason in the Plantation of Virginia for the
    term of four years as a carpenter and joiner.

    2. Buckland represents to Mason that Buckland is twenty-two years
    old, single, and not contracted to serve any other person.

    3. Mason will, at Mason's own cost, carry and convey Buckland to
    the Plantation.

    4. Mason will, at Mason's own cost, provide necessary meat, drink,
    washing, and lodging for Buckland.

    5. Mason will pay Buckland wages at the rate of twenty pounds
    sterling per annum payable quarterly.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
    Executed as of the date above.

That is much clearer than it was, isn't it? It turns out the agreement was
very simple and straight forward. But the legalese was so excessive it
obscured the substance of the agreement. To avoid that happening to the
documents you draft, stick with modern English. Use the language people
use today, not the language people used 300 years ago.

H. Avoid Excessive Redundancy

Another thing to watch out for is excessively redundant phrases in contract
drafting. One place you will commonly see them is in a Release of Claims.
Instead of just describing the releasing party as, for example, the
"Corporation" the document will add "on its own behalf and on behalf of its
subsidiaries and other affiliates, and its and their respective representatives
and the respective heirs, executors, administrators, successors and assigns
of each of the foregoing." And instead of just saying the Corporation
releases the other party of whatever "claims" it may have, the document
will say the Corporation releases the other party from "past, present or
future claims, rights, orders, causes of action, suits, liabilities, debts, dues,
sums of money, accounts, actions, reckonings, bonds, bills, specialties,
covenants, contracts, controversies, counterclaims, cross-claims, defenses,
obligations, promises, costs, damages, judgments, extents, executions,
losses and demands of any kind, nature or description in any forum
whatsoever."

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Lawyers draft that way when they are more concerned with being thorough
than being clear. They want to make sure they have covered every
conceivable situation so that their client is fully and completely protected.
But sometimes they go too far and end up creating a provision that is
wordy, awkward and ambiguous. Contrary to popular belief, lawyers don't
get paid by the word. So also try to avoid excessively redundant phrases
like these whenever practicable.

I. Avoid Passive Voice

Avoid passive voice when drafting contract provisions. If you write "the
house will be painted red by the Seller," you are putting the focus on the
house. But that's not what you want to do. You want to put the focus on
the Seller, not the house. You are assigning the obligation to a person not a
thing. And you do that by using active voice. In other words, you do that
by writing it this way: "the Seller will paint the house red." Drafting an
obligation, right, or prohibition, by starting with the name of the party you
are obligating, empowering, or prohibiting ensures you don't run afoul of
any this rule or the preceding two rules.

The only exception I can think of is for the exordium and the testimonium.
When drafting either of those descriptive statements, it is acceptable to put
the focus on the document, not the parties, since both of those statements
describe the execution of the document itself, not the provisions of the
agreement between the parties.

J. Write Numbers Consistently

There are three common ways to write a number in a contract. You can
write the number numerically. You can write the number in words. Or you
can write the number in words and also numerically in parenthesis, like this:

                                            30
                                          Thirty

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
                                       Thirty (30)

You have probably noticed I have been writing numbers out in words, then
putting the numeral in parenthesis in this book. There is a simple reason I
got into the habit of doing it that way, and that is because I have found it is
harder to make a mistake when you write a number out. Writing
$1,917,432 instead of $1,197,432 seems like a relatively minor typo. I just
transposed the 9 and the 1. But that little typo could result in a $720,000
malpractice claim, if it isn't corrected before the document is signed.

Spelling plaintiff with three (3) f's in an appellate brief, might cause a little
damage to your reputation, but doing that will not likely cause any
monetary harm. In contrast, typos involving numbers in contracts,
especially big dollar numbers, can have a direct adverse monetary impact
on the drafter. That is why I prefer to spell numbers out, especially when I
am referring to large dollar amounts.

Also, in practice I primarily handled finance transactions, and dollar
amounts in promissory notes are always written out (like they are written in
checks). So, instead of writing $100,000, I would write the number out,
like this:

 One hundred thousand and 00/100 dollars ($100,000.00).

But I also recognize, no one way of writing numbers is any more or less
clear than any other. In other words, how you choose to write numbers in
an agreement is another matter of style. So just be consistent with how you
chose to do it.

In summary, pay a extra attention to time periods to make sure you
articulate them clearly. Be clear what the trigger date is, how long the
duration is in days, and whether the last day is included. Make sure
modifiers are as close as possible to the words or phrases they modify,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
especially when you articulate a condition. Solve the indefinite pronoun
reference problem by not using them at all. Don't leave the party out of
obligations, rights, and prohibitions; and don't put nonparties in obligations,
rights, and prohibitions. Avoid ambiguous phrases like and/or and etc.
Watch out for excessive legalese and redundancy in contract provisions.
Draft in active voice and write numbers consistently. Also, pay extra
attention to large dollar amounts. Lawyers are trained to pay close attention
to language, but it is the numbers that can really get you into trouble.

Now that we have covered how to clearly and unambiguously draft
obligations, right, prohibitions and descriptive statements, and what
potentially ambiguous words and phrases to avoid, we will next talk about
how to organize the provisions of a contract. Sentences are made clear by
keeping them simple, accurately and precisely describing factual
information, and drafting the same provisions the same way. A contract is
made clear by grouping and ordering the contract's provisions in an
organized way. That is the subject of the next few chapters.

                                  Practice Exercises
             Correct the errors in the following contract provisions:
 1. The Buyer will pay the Seller $100,000 (one hundred thousand
 dollars) within a week after the completion aforementioned Project.
 2. If the Buyer desires to inspect the Property, they will use their
 inspector. Notice of the approval of said inspection will be provided by
 the Buyer prior to Closing.
 3. Whereas, notice will be provided by document, paper, writing, hard
 copy and/or physical form, etc., no other communication, signal, utterance
 or notification shall be herewith required.
 4. After approval the Seller's employees will construct the Project. Only
 materials listed in the Specifications will be used, unless the Buyer

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
notifies the Seller of her waiver of any particular specification and her
approval of any substitute material.
5. If the Buyer fails to pay the Seller by a month the Buyer will pay the
Seller a late fee of in the amount of $25.00.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
4. ORGANIZING A
CONTRACT'S BEGINNING
SECTIONS

A greements typically start with a title, then an introductory
            paragraph that identifies the parties, the document, and the date.
            There is no requirement to include a background section, but it is
often helpful to include one. In a short document, definitions are usually

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
created in context; but in a longer document, when you have a lot of
definitions, it is often advisable to include a definitions section. The same
is true of representations and warranties. If you have a lot, it would be best
to create a section for them and put the section near the beginning of the
agreement. Those are the beginning sections of a contract. In this chapter I
will discuss how to draft each section clearly and unambiguously.

A. Identify the Type of Agreement in the Title

Contracts typically start with a title describing the type of agreement
involved. If you are drafting a lease agreement for the rental of a residential
apartment, don't just title it "Agreement." That doesn't distinguish it from
any other agreement. And don't write a title that goes too far in the other
direction, like this: "Agreement between Landlord Corp. and Tammy
Tenant Concerning the Rental of the Apartment at 123 Main Street in
Gainesville, Florida." That's too much information. Most of it belongs in
the body of the agreement, not the title. Just identify the type of agreement,
like this:

                               APARTMENT LEASE

I capitalized, centered, and underlined the title to make it stand out. You
can also use bold to make a title (or a heading) stand out in an agreement
(or some other combination of one or more of those four techniques). But
don't do things like italicizing letters, making the font bigger, or using any
type of graphics. That will just make the agreement look tacky. Keep it
professional!

B. Start with an Exordium (Preamble or Introduction)

After the title there should be an introductory section called the
"exordium." ("Exordium" is Latin for "a beginning" or "commencement."
Lewis & Short, Latin Dictionary.) The exordium is a description of what
the parties are doing (entering into a contract). It contains the date of the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
agreement, the identity of the parties, and the title of the document, as well
as short-hand references for the parties and the document. There are two
ways to do it. One is passive voice and puts the emphasis on the document,
like this:

 This Apartment Lease (Lease) is entered into on this___ day of
 ____________, 20__, by and between Landlord Corp., a Florida
 corporation, having an office at 57 West Street, Gainesville, Florida
 (Landlord) and Tammy Tenant of 75 First Street, Gainesville, Florida
 (Tenant).

The other is active voice and puts the emphasis on the parties, like this:

 On ___________ ___, 20__, Landlord Corp., a Florida corporation,
 having an office at 57 West Street, Gainesville, Florida (Landlord)
 and Tammy Tenant of 75 First Street, Gainesville, Florida (Tenant)
 enter into this Apartment Lease (Lease).

I personally prefer the active voice form, but many attorneys also use the
other one. What is important in drafting an exordium is not so much the
form you use, but the information you include and the way you express that
information. To identify a person, you need the person's full name and
address. To identify an entity like a corporation, you need the entity's name
and address, but you also need to know the state in which the entity was
organized. This information is important for a number of reasons.

First and foremost, a contract entered into with the wrong party or
something that is not an actual party, is worthless. If you start a real estate
purchase agreement by stating "Lancaster Williams" is the seller, but title to
the property is actually held by "Lancaster Family Trust," you have made a
serious mistake. If you are drafting an agreement for the purchase of
business assets and you state the seller is "Frannie's Pizza," your contract is
no good. "Frannie's Pizza" may be the name of the business but it is not a

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
person or entity. You must state the name of the owner, Frannie Salpietro,
and then you can add "d/b/a Frannie's Pizza."

Second, you must include addresses to further identify the parties and
enable them to provide notice to each other. A loan agreement may require
a lender to give a borrower notice of default, so it is helpful if the
agreement says where the borrower is located. If the same agreement is
breached and the lender wants to sue the borrower, it will, again, be helpful
to know where the borrower can be served with the summons and
complaint.

When you draft the exordium, you must express this information
accurately. Make sure you have the party names correct. Also, when you
refer to the document the parties are entering into, make sure you describe it
accurately. Don't say the parties "enter into this rental agreement" if you
have titled the contract "apartment lease." State the parties "enter into this
apartment lease."

Finally, if the date of the agreement is different than the date it is signed,
date the agreement "as of" the effective date in the exordium, or define the
"Effective Date" in the body of the agreement.

After the exordium, you need to insert what are called "words of
agreement." In older agreements, you would do that by saying something
like "Now, therefore, in consideration of the mutual covenants herein
contained, the parties agree as follows" or "Now, therefore, for good and
valuable consideration the sufficiency of which is hereby acknowledged,
the parties agree to the following." But today the preferred practice is to
leave out the legalese and the recitation of consideration and just add the
following sentence to the end of the exordium: "The parties mutually agree
as follows." Alternatively, If a background section is included (discussed
below), it is common to add "Accordingly, the parties agree as follows"
after the background section. Either way is fine.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
C. Consider Including a Background Section

After the exordium you may want to include a background section. A
contract does not have to have a background section, but it is often helpful
to include one. For example, you may want to explain the events that led
up to the creation of the contract or the context within which the contract is
signed. For example, if you are drafting an employment agreement, you
could do something like this:

 I. Background of Agreement

 A. Search for Employees. The Employer conducted a nationwide
 search for qualified trial attorneys by posting an advertisement in the
 journal of the American Bar Association.

 B. Submission of Resume. The Employee responded to the
 advertisement by submitting a current resume listing the Employee's
 education, employment and outside affiliations (Resume), a true and
 accurate copy of which is attached to this Agreement as Exhibit A.

 C. Offer of Employment. The Employer interviewed the Employee
 and offered the Employee employment; and the Employee accepted
 the offer.

I also used this section to authenticate the employee's resume, and I created
a short-hand term for that document. I did that so I would have a simple but
accurate way to refer to the resume in the body of the agreement. For
example, I might want the employee to represent to the employer that the
