statements in the Resume are truthful. If it turns out the employee was
lying, that would constitute a breach, enabling the employer to end the
relationship.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Another reason you may want to do a background section is to describe the
parties and the subject matter of the agreement. For example, if you were
drafting an equipment lease, you might do something like this:

 I. Background of Agreement

 A. Business of Food Service Corp. Food Service Corp. is in the
 business of operating food service kiosks in shopping malls in
 Northern Central Florida.

 B. Equipment for Kiosks. Food Service Corp. has determined that
 it is in its best interests to lease, rather than purchase the kitchen
 equipment for its food kiosks.

 C. Business of Supply Corp. Supply Corp. is in the business of
 leasing commercial kitchen equipment to restaurants and other food
 service providers in Florida.

 D. Equipment for Lease. This Agreement concerns the following
 pieces of equipment: one Vulcan VC44GD - Double Deck Gas
 Convection Oven, Mfg Part#: VC44GD, one Anets SLG100 -
 Commercial Gas Fryer, 70 to 100 Lb. Oil Capacity, Mfg Part#:
 SLG100, one True T-49F - Commercial Freezer - Two Door - Reach-
 In - 49 Cu. Ft., Mfg Part#: T-49F-HC and three Kratos TT-244 -
 Stainless Steel Work Tables with Galvanized Undershelf,
 48"Wx24"D, Mfg Part#: KRA-4824 (collectively, the Equipment).

I used this background section primarily to describe the subject matter of
the contract, which is the kitchen equipment, and create a short-hand
reference for the Equipment. What is most important in this section is that
the description is accurate and complete. Later in the agreement I will have
an obligation stating something like: "Supply Corp. will lease the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Equipment to Food Service Corp. for $_________." If the definition is not
accurate and complete, that obligation will not work, and neither will any of
the other obs and provisions involving the Equipment.

I was also very specific when I described the Equipment. If I had said the
Equipment consisted of "an oven, a fryer, a freezer and three (3)
worktables," the description would have been ambiguous. So, another way
to achieve clarity is by being specific when you describe something,
especially something important like the subject matter of the contract.

D. Use Short-Hand References (Definitions)

After the background section you may also want to include a section listing
all the defined terms in the agreement. The phrase "defined terms" is a
misnomer. What we are really talking about is short-hand references; but
they are commonly referred to as definitions. To this point, I have been
defining terms within each sentence as they came up. Doing it that way, the
convention is to describe the item in detail then put a short-hand reference
in parenthesis, capitalizing the first letter of each word (you can also put
quotation marks before and after the reference, but it is not necessary to do
that). So, if you had a statement describing a car in a purchase and sale
contract, it might look like this:

 The subject matter of this Agreement is a 1963 Ferrari 250 GTO
 having the chassis number 4153 GT (the Car).

Again, you could put the definition in quotes, like this: (the "Car"). Either
way is fine; just be consistent. But don't put the definition before the
description, put it after. So, in the example above, don't say "the subject
matter of this Agreement (the Car) is a 1963 Ferrari 250 GTO with chassis
number 4153 GT." Put the definition, (the Car), at the end of the
description.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
The reason for doing this is to be absolutely clear you are referring to the
most expensive car ever sold: the 1963 Ferrari 250 GTO, with the chassis
number 4153 GT (the car that won the Tour de France), whenever you refer
to the Car in the agreement. You don't want to have to repeat all that
information every time you refer to the Car, but you still want to make sure
your references to it are clear, accurate, and complete. And the definition
accomplishes that result.

When you are drafting definitions, avoid including rights, obligations and
prohibitions in your definitions. They should all just be descriptive
statements. And avoid unnecessary definitions. For example, it is
appropriate to define something like this:

 This Contract concerns the sale of the land with the building thereon
 located at 51 West Street in Miami, Florida consisting of 3 acres and a
 3,000 square foot 3 bedroom, 2 bathroom home with attached 2 car
 garage (the Property) .

But it is unnecessary and inappropriate to define things like this:

 The Company is in the business of selling greeting cards (Greeting Cards)
 to customers in central Florida (Customers) at retail establishments
 (Establishments) in local shopping malls (Malls).

In fact, if you are drafting a simple contract, it is often only appropriate to
define the parties, the subject matter and the agreement itself. So be careful
not to over do it with definitions!

If you have multiple terms in an agreement, it is sometimes better to put all
the definitions in one place, usually at the beginning of the agreement, right
after the background section. The convention is to alphabetize the list of
terms, put each term in quotes instead of parenthesis, capitalize the first
letter, use the word "means," and then state a detailed description. Here is
an example from a loan agreement:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 II. Definitions of Terms

 The following terms have the following meanings:

 "Loan" means the loan of $100,000.00 to the Borrower by the Lender
 pursuant to this Agreement.

 "Loan Documents" means i) the Note; (ii) the Mortgage; and (iii) this
 Agreement.

 "Mortgage" means the Mortgage of even date from the Borrower to
 the Lender granting the Lender a mortgage on the Mortgaged
 Property.

 "Mortgaged Property" means the land, together with all the buildings
 and improvements on the land, situated at 123 Main Street in
 Gainesville, Florida.

 "Note" means the Promissory Note of even date made by the
 Borrower in the amount of One Hundred Thousand Dollars
 ($100,000.00), payable to the order of the Lender.

I didn't define the terms Lender, Borrower or Agreement in the list because
those terms would have already been defined in the exordium.

The important thing is that the presentation is clear, accurate, and complete.
If the actual loan amount is $1,000,000 not $100,000 then it doesn't matter
what form is used. The document has a problem. If there is a security
agreement in addition to the mortgage, then the definition of the Loan
Documents, and all the references to it in the loan agreement, will not

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
accomplish their intended purpose. The same thing is true if there is a typo
in the property address. It is important to learn the forms that are typically
used for drafting contracts, but not at the expense of substance. Ultimately,
it is the substance that matters most.

E. List Representations and Warranties, if Applicable

After the definitions section, if there is one, you will on occasion want to
include one more section; and that is a statement of the representations and
warranties a party relied on in entering into the contract. Like definitions,
representations and warranties can just be included within each provision as
they come up. For example, in the background section of the employment
agreement mentioned earlier, you might add a representation, like this:

*  *  *

C. Submission of Resume. The Employee responded to the
advertisement by submitting a current resume listing his education,
employment, and outside affiliations (Resume), a true and accurate
copy of which is attached to this Agreement as Exhibit A.

D. Accuracy of Information. The Employee represents and
warrants to the Employer that the information in the Resume is
truthful and accurate.

However, if you have multiple representations and warranties you want to
include in an agreement, it is usually better to put them all in one place,
right after the background and definitions sections. Just make it clear who
is making the representations and to whom they are being made. And since
these are descriptive statements, use present or past tense. Here is an
example from a purchase and sale agreement:

   Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 III. Representations and Warranties of Seller

 The Seller makes the following representations and warranties to the
 Buyer:

 1. Pumping of Septic. The Seller arranged for the pumping of the
 septic on the Property once per year for every year the Seller has
 owned the Property.

 2. Potability of Water. The water from the well on the Property is
 potable.

 3. Permits for Improvements. The Seller obtained a permit for all
 improvements done to the Property.

 4. Storage or Disposal of Waste. The Seller did not store or
 dispose of any hazardous wastes on the Property.

You need to be careful not to over do representations and warranties by
including what should be background statements (e.g. descriptions of the
subject matter of the agreement), obligations, rights and prohibitions. Here
are examples statements incorrectly listed as representations and warranties:

 III. Representations and Warranties of Seller

 The Seller makes the following representations and warranties to the
 Buyer:

 1. Features of Car. The Car is a black four dour sedan with a six
 cylinder engine, and four wheel disc brakes.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
2. Sale of Car. The Seller will sell the car to the Buyer.

3. Inspection of Car. The Buyer may inspect the car before the
Closing.

4. Use of Car by Seller. After this Agreement is signed the Seller
will not use drive the car for any distance, except in connection with
the inspection.

After the performance of a purchase agreement (i.e. after the closing
occurs) the substantive rights and obligations of the parties end. If the
purpose of the representations is to give the party receiving them the right
to cancel the agreement if it turns out they are not true, then they are fine
the way they are. But if you want the party receiving the representation to
be able to take legal action if that party discovers the representation is false
after the transaction closes, then you need to state in the agreement that the
representation "survives" the closing, like this:

*  *  *

2. Potability of Water. The water from the well on the Property is
potable. This representation and warranty survives the closing of
title under this Agreement.

*  *  *

F. Add Headings to Sections and Subsections

As you can see, I started each of these sections with a heading describing
the content. When you change to a new topic, you should add a heading

Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
before you start. And when you draft the heading, consider the same issues
discussed at the beginning of this chapter on titles. Try to avoid one-word
headings, like "Payment." Ask yourself "who," "what," "when," "where,"
or "how," depending upon which applies. So, in this case, ask, payment for
what? And use the heading "Payment for Rent." Instead of "Restrictions,"
use "Restrictions on Use." Instead of "Termination," use "Termination of
Services."

Also, if you have multiple subsections under the heading, number them (or
use letters). As long as the subsections are not just an itemized list, add
headings to the subsections as well. Headings are like pepperoni slices on
pizza; there is no such thing as too many. The only exception is if you just
have one subsection. In that case, don't number or letter it, or give it a
separate heading. Only add headings to subsections if you have two or
more subsections.

You will also notice I broke the text up in each of these sections instead of
grouping it all into one paragraph. These sections are short enough that
either way would have probably been fine. On the one hand, you shouldn't
feel like every sentence in an agreement has to be in a separate section.
Grouping like provisions is a good practice to get into. On the other hand,
if your section is getting bigger than about half a page, it is probably time to
break it up.

To recap, a contract typically starts out with a title and an exordium. To
complete the exordium, you will need the name, address, and state of
organization of the parties. Next is a background section, which you can
use to describe the events leading up to the contract, or the parties and the
subject matter. If it is a sale of goods contract, describe the goods
involved. If it is a services contract, describe the services.

A short agreement will not need a definitions section, but a lengthy
document containing a lot of defined terms may need one. Representations
and warranties can also be included in the background section or in the
body of the agreement if there are only a few of them. But in a longer,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
more complex document, involving a long list of representations, the better
practice is to include a representations and warranties section. Those are
the sections you will typically find in the beginning of contracts. Follow
the advice in this chapter to draft each section clearly and without
ambiguity.

                                  Practice Exercises
           Begin drafting the Associate Employment Agreement, Laptop
 Purchase Agreement, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of Assets
 Agreement and/or Consulting Services Agreement you chose for the
 practice exercise at the end of the previous chapter.
         Draft the beginning sections of the contract(s) by determining what
 information is properly included in the title, exordium, background
 section, definitions and representations and warranties, if applicable, and
 then drafting each of those provisions.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
5. DRAFTING THE CORE
OBLIGATIONS AND
ORGANIZING DEAL
PROVISIONS

ow that we have gone through title, exordium, background, definitions and
representations, and warranties sections, we can finally get into the meat of

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Nthe agreement: the core obligations and duration, if applicable. Then the
            details of the deal, organized by substance or chronologically,
            whichever is clearer.

A. Start with the Core of the Agreement

Agreements typically consist of an exchange of bilateral promises. For an
agreement to work, the drafter needs to obligate both sides to do what they
agreed to do. Usually, the best practice is to start broad and then get more
specific. That means the agreement should start with the core obligations: a
statement of the basic exchange between the parties.

I will go over three types of agreements; each involves a different core
exchange. The first type is a term agreement that governs the relationship
of the parties over time. An example of that is an apartment lease or
employment agreement. An agreement like that will need a statement of
duration or term in addition to the core obligations.

The second type is a purchase and sale agreement, like a real estate
purchase and sale agreement, or an agreement for the purchase of a business
or equipment. A purchase and sale agreement will usually describe a due
diligence period, and then a closing when the real estate or goods is
exchanged for the purchase price.

And the third type of agreement is one that involves a transfer of rights, like
a mortgage, an assignment of leases and rents, or a software license
agreement. I'll give you examples of all three types of core provisions.

The core obligations for a lease might look like this:

 IV. Lease of Property
 The Landlord will lease the Property to the Tenant; and the Tenant
 will lease the Property from the Landlord.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Or this:

 IV. Lease of Property
 The Landlord will lease the Property to the Tenant; and the Tenant
 will pay the Landlord rent as provided in this Agreement.

The first example obligates the tenant to lease the property but doesn't
mention rent, while the second example does. If you do it the first way, you
will have an additional obligation requiring the tenant to pay rent. After
both versions you will want to subsequently state things like what the rent
amount is, when it is due, and how it is to be paid. You can put some of
those details into the core obligations, as long as it doesn't get unwieldy.
But again, the idea is to state the basic exchange first, and then get into the
details later.

Here is an example of the core obligations for a purchase and sale
agreement, which is an agreement that involves a transaction or exchange
after a due diligence period:

 IV. Sale of Condominium
 The Seller will sell the Condominium to the Buyer; and the Buyer will
 purchase the Condominium from the Seller for the Purchase Price on
 the Closing Date.

I was able to draft these obligations very concisely because I used a few
defined terms. As long as the definitions are also accurate and clear, the
obligations should work to bind both sides to do what they agreed to do.

And here is one more example. This one is from a mortgage and security
agreement. In that type of agreement, there usually is no core exchange of
obligations. Rather, the core of the agreement is simply a transfer of rights,
like this:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 IV. Grant of Mortgage
 The Borrower mortgages, grants, and conveys the Property to the
 Lender as security for the Loan.

I didn't use the word "will" here because this is not an obligation. The
Borrower is not going to mortgage the Property sometime in the future.
The mortgage is granted when the agreement is signed. The core of a this
type of agreement is actually a descriptive statement, describing what the
party is doing (e.g., transferring ownership, granting rights, releasing
liability).

B. Describe the Duration or Term, if Applicable

If you are drafting a term agreement, you will have to describe the term
(how long the relationship will last). Also, since the term may not start
right when the contract is signed, it is advisable to state the beginning and
ending dates. Here is an example of a duration provision in an agreement:

 V. Term of Rental
 The rental period is for three (3) years, beginning on September 1,
 2020 and ending on August 30, 2020 (the Term).

If the term renews, then you would add a statement to that effect, like "the
Term renews automatically unless either party sends the other party a notice
of non-renewal at least thirty (30) days before the end of the Term."

C. Then Draft the Specifics of the Deal

Once you have drafted the core obligations, and a duration provision, if
applicable, spell out in detail the specific terms of the deal with a series of
additional obligations, rights and prohibitions, as applicable. Obviously, the
sections of the agreement will differ depending on whether the agreement is
for a purchase and sale, like an agreement to buy real estate, or a

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
relationship over time, like a lease, or if the agreement involves a transfer of
rights, like a mortgage.

       (1) Purchase and Sale Agreement

If the document is a contract for the purchase and sale of a condominium,
for example, the seller would be required to produce the condo docs,
provide the buyer with a title insurance commitment, and draft a settlement
statement before the closing date. As a result, the provisions following the
core obligations might look like this:

 V. Organizational Documents for Condominium

 On or before the seventh (7th) day after this Agreement is signed, the
 Seller will provide the Buyer with the condominium documents for
 the Property, including the master deed, declaration of trust, and
 bylaws.

 IV. Insurance Policy for Title

 No later than fourteen (14) days before the Closing Date, the Seller
 will provide the Buyer with a title insurance commitment issued by a
 Florida licensed title insurer, with legible copies of instruments listed
 as exceptions.

 VI. Statement of Closing Costs

 At least twenty-four (24) hours before the Closing Date, the Seller will
 provide the Buyer with a completed Settlement Statement for the
 closing, detailing all costs and charges to be incurred by the Buyer
 and the Seller in connection with the sale of the Property.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Similarly, the buyer might be obligated to apply for financing for the
purchase, have the right to inspect the property, and have the right to a final
"walk through" before the closing occurs. As a result, a series of additional
provisions might look like this:

 VII. Inspection of Property

 On or before the fifteenth (15th) day after this Agreement is signed,
 the Buyer may inspect the Property. If the Buyer determines that the
 Property is not acceptable to the Buyer, the Buyer may terminate this
 Agreement by delivering written notice of such termination to the
 Seller prior to the expiration of this inspection period.

 VIII. Application for Financing

 On or before the thirtieth (30th) day after this Agreement is signed,
 the Buyer will apply for financing for the purchase of the Property,
 and use diligent efforts to obtain approval for such financing, in the
 amount of no more than $___________ at an initial interest rate of no
 less than %____ for a term of no more than _____ years. If the Buyer
 is unable to obtain such approval after the exercise of diligent effort,
 then the Buyer may terminate this Agreement by delivering written
 notice of such termination to the Seller prior to the expiration of this
 financing period.

 IX. Walk Through Before Closing

 On the day prior to the Closing Date, the Buyer may perform a walk-
 through inspection of the Property to confirm all items of personal
 property purchased by the Buyer have remained on the Property and
 Seller has maintained the Property as required in this Agreement.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
       (2) Term Agreement

If the agreement is for a relationship over time, like an equipment lease,
then the core obligations might be followed by provisions relating to
shipment, use, maintenance, and access, like this:

 I. Shipment of Equipment

 The Retailer will arrange for the shipment of the Landscaping
 Equipment to the Office Complex on the first day of the Term, and
 the shipment of the Landscaping Equipment back to the Retailer on
 the last day of the Term. 

 II. Use of Equipment

 The Office Complex will use the Equipment in a safe and appropriate
 manner at all times. The Office Complex may permit its employees to
 use the Landscaping Equipment, if the employees have received
 appropriate training.

 III. Maintenance of Equipment

 The Office Complex will perform routine maintenance of the
 Landscaping Equipment, in accordance with the owner's manual for
 each piece of the Landscaping Equipment, during the Term of this
 Agreement.

 IV. Access to Equipment

 The Retailer may inspect the Landscaping Equipment at the Office
 Complex any time from 8:00 a.m. through 5:00 p.m., Monday
 through Friday.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
       (3) Agreement for Transfer of Rights

If the agreement involves a transfer of rights, like a mortgage, then the core
transfer may be followed by requirements for the payment of indebtedness
and taxes, maintaining the property and repairing damage, and complying
with leases, like this:

 1.1 Compliance with Loan Documents

 The Borrower will perform and observe all of the terms and
 conditions of the Loan Documents, including the obligation to make
 any payments as required in the Loan Documents.

 1.2 Payment of Taxes

 The Borrower will pay or cause to be paid all taxes, water, sewer and
 other utility charges, and all other charges or assessments relating to
 the Property or the materials stored on or in the Property and other
 charges and encumbrances which are or may become a lien on the
 Property, prior to the time when interest or penalties would accrue on
 such amounts.

 1.3 Maintenance of Property

 The Borrower will keep the Property in good order, repair and
 condition, reasonable wear and tear excepted, and will not permit,
 commit or suffer any waste, impairment, deterioration or
 environmental contamination of the Property.

 1.4 Repair of Damage

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 The Borrower will promptly repair, restore, rebuild, replace, or alter
 as necessary any portion of the Property which may be damaged or
 destroyed by fire or other casualty, as nearly as possible to the
 condition such improvements were in prior to such damage or
 destruction. The Borrower will give the Lender notice of any damage
 to the Property no later than five (5) business days after any such
 damage occurs.

 1.5 Compliance with Leases

 The Borrower will comply with and observe its obligations as
 landlord under leases affecting the Property.

Depending upon the circumstances, it may be best to organize the
provisions of the contract by substance. For example, you may organize an
agreement with all the payment provisions in one section, and additional
rights and obligations with respect to the services in another section.

But there may also be situations where it works best to organize provisions
chronologically. For example, in the condominium purchase and sale
agreement mentioned earlier, the seller produces the condominium
documents first, and then the buyer inspects the property. After that, the
buyer applies for financing. If it is approved, the seller generates a
settlement statement and the Buyer does a final walkthrough, and then the
final closing is held.

Start with a general statement of the core exchange and then fill in the
details as you work your way through the document. If a chronological
organization makes sense, then organize the supplemental provisions
chronologically. If something else works better, do something else. Clarity
is the goal so whatever expresses the specifics of the deal in the clearest
way is the structure you should use.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001

                                  Practice Exercises

           Continue drafting the Associate Employment Agreement, Laptop
 Purchase Agreement, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of Assets
 Agreement and/or Consulting Services Agreement you have been drafting
 as the practice exercise for the previous chapter.

         Draft the middle sections of the contract(s) by determining what
 information is properly included in the core obligations, duration, if
 applicable, and deal provisions, and then drafting each of those
 provisions.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
6. ORGANIZING A
CONTRACT'S ENDING
SECTIONS

T he ending sections of a contract usually deal with the end of the
          relationship. If the contract has a term, you should think about
          whether it is appropriate to include a termination provision or a
cancellation provision. If the contract involves a closing, you will want to

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
describe the logistics for making that happen, as well as dealing with the
situation where the closing falls through. If the document is something like
a security agreement, you may just need to provide for the termination of
the security interest at the end of the relationship. A list of administrative
provisions (a/k/a "boilerplate") is usually included next, then a final
concluding statement (a "testimonium") and signature blocks.

A. If the Contract is a Term Agreement, Draft Provisions for Ending it
Early

If the contract has a term or duration, then you will likely want to draft
provisions for ending the relationship voluntarily by either cancelling or
terminating it. Many attorneys use those terms interchangeably but there is
a distinction between the two you should be aware of in the Uniform
Commercial Code. Here is the relevant provision in the Florida version of
the Code:

*  *  *

(3)"Termination" occurs when either party pursuant to a power
created by agreement or law puts an end to the contract otherwise than
for its breach. On termination, all obligations which are still executory on
both sides are discharged but any right based on prior breach or
performance survives.

 (4)"Cancellation" occurs when either party puts an end to the contract
 for breach by the other and its effect is the same as that of "termination"
 except that the canceling party also retains any remedy for breach of the
 whole contract or any unperformed balance.
 Fla. Stat. § 672.106.

Cancellation is ending the contract because the other party breached;
termination is ending the contract for any other reason. Both cancellation

   Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
and termination are grants of rights. So, if you were giving the lessor in an
equipment lease the right to cancel, you would do it like this:

 V. Cancellation by Lessor

 If the Lessee breaches any of the provisions of the Lease, the Lessor
 may cancel.
Like any other provision, you want to make sure it is complete. In this
case, what is missing is how the lessor would go about cancelling the lease.

 V. Cancellation by Lessor

 If the Lessee breaches any of the provisions of the Lease, the Lessor
 may cancel by giving the Lessee written notice.

Termination is similar. If you wanted to give the lessee the right to
terminate, you might draft something like this:
 VI. Termination by Lessee

 The Lessee may terminate the Lease for any reason.

But again, you would need to say how termination is accomplished. And
since termination can happen at any time for any reason, you would likely
want to add a time deadline.
 VI. Termination by Lessee

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 The Lessee may terminate the Lease for any reason by giving written
 notice to the Lessor no later than thirty (30) days before the proposed
 termination date.

B. If a Security Deposit was Required, Provide for its Return

In a term agreement, like an apartment rental agreement, there may be a
security deposit involved. If there is, then in addition to ending the
relationship, the contract will also have to deal with the ultimate disposition
of deposited funds. For a security deposit, you will at least need an
obligation requiring the tenant to pay the deposit at the beginning of the
agreement, then a provision at the end giving the landlord the right to
deduct amounts for damages, and then requiring the landlord to return the
balance, like this:

 VI. Disposition of Security Deposit

 The Landlord may deduct from the Security Deposit costs and
 expenses incurred by the Landlord to repair damage to the Unit
 caused by the Tenant during the Term. The Landlord will send the
 Tenant the balance of the Security Deposit no later than thirty (30)
 days after the end of the Term.

C. If the Contract is a Purchase and Sale Agreement, Draft Provisions
for Closing Logistics

If you are drafting an agreement for a purchase of real estate or goods, you
will need to describe the time and place of closing, as well as all the things
each party is required to bring to the closing for the transaction to happen.
Those things are sometimes called "closing deliveries." Here is a short
example:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 V. Closing of Sale

 A. The closing is to occur at 10:00 a.m. at the offices of Dewey,
 Cheatham, and Howe, 999 Court Street, Alachua, Florida (the
 "Closing").

 B. At the Closing, the Seller will execute and deliver to the Buyer the
 following documents:

     (i) a quitclaim deed for the Property;
     (ii) keys to all doors on the Property and the post office box for
 the Property;
     (iii) access information for smart locks, garage doors, gates and
 fences on the Property;
     (iv) remote control devices for appliances and other systems on
 the Property; and
     (v) operating manuals and warranties for all appliances on the
 Property.

 C. At the Closing, the Buyer will deliver to the Seller, by wire
 transfer to the Seller's account, One Hundred Twenty-Five Thousand
 Dollars ($125,000.00) in immediately available funds.

D. If the Security Deposit Serves as Liquidated Damages in the Event of
a Breach, Provide for its Disposition in That Situation as Well.

An agreement for a sale often involves a deposit which serves as liquidated
damages for one of the parties if the other fails to complete the transaction.
If it does, the agreement should also provide for the ultimate disposition of
the deposit if the transaction falls through. For example, a purchase and
sale agreement for real estate will often provide as follows:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 VI. Retention of Deposit

 If the Buyer fails to fulfill the Buyer's obligations in this Agreement,
 the Seller may retain all deposits made by the Buyer under this
 Agreement as liquidated damages.

E. If the Contract Involves a Transfer of Rights, There May be Nothing
to Do

An agreement involving a transfer of rights is performed when it is signed,
so there may be no need to provide for the end of the relationship. Still,
something like a mortgage might have a provision obligating the mortgagee
to execute and record a discharge when the underlying loan is paid. Also, it
is possible for this type of agreement to have a term. For example, a
software license agreement could be for a limited period of time. If that
were the case, you would consider adding termination and/or cancellation
provisions, if appropriate. Otherwise, you may not have to deal with how
the relationship ends.

F. End with Descriptions of Administrative Policies

The administrative policy statements that are usually included at the end of
all types of agreements are often called "boilerplate" provisions. It is
unfortunate they have that nickname because it understates their
importance. Administrative provisions can have a dramatic effect on the
rights and obligations of the parties. The most basic of these are merger,
modification, choice of law, and choice of forum or venue.

A merger or integration provision states that the contract is complete; and
any prior agreements not included in the agreement are unenforceable. A

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
merger provision is a descriptive statement, not an obligation, grant of
rights, or prohibition. Here is an example:

 XIII. Integration of Agreement

 This Agreement is the complete and final agreement between the
 parties regarding the sale of the Business. All prior statements,
 understandings, and agreements are merged into this Agreement.

A merger clause in a contract simply states the parties' intent with regard to
integration. 11 Williston on Contracts § 33:23 (4th ed.). If the agreement
actually is integrated, the parol evidence rule renders inoperative prior
written or oral agreements. Restatement (Second) of Contracts § 213
(1981).

I think of a "modification" provision as the corollary to a merger provision.
A merger provision deals with statements and agreements made before the
contract is signed. A modification provision deals with agreements made
after the contract is signed. A modification provision is not a grant of rights;
the parties already have the right to modify the contract. It could be drafted
as a condition to an obligation. "If the parties want to modify, they will
only modify in writing." Or, it could be a prohibition. "The parties will not
modify orally." But drafting it that way does not really accomplish the
desired effect. You don't want to sue the other party for breach; you want
the modification to be invalid. For that reason, I would draft the provision
as another policy statement, like this:

 XIV. Modification of Agreement

 Any amendment of this Agreement is invalid unless it is in writing,
 signed by both parties, and states the nature of the modification, and
 the provision to which it applies.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
An agreement for the sale of goods cannot be modified orally if it contains
a modification provision. Uniform Commercial Code § 2-209(2) (2002) ("A
signed agreement which excludes modification or rescission except by a
signed writing cannot be otherwise modified or rescinded"). However, any
other type of written contract can likely be orally modified, even if it
contains such a provision, if it is proven that the provision was orally
waived. 10 Williston on Contracts § 29:42 (4th ed.).

Choice of law is another provision that can only be done as a descriptive
statement. It is not an obligation; you can't obligate the judge to apply a
specific state's law. And it is not a grant of rights or a prohibition. Often a
choice of law provision is drafted just to make clear what law applies to
breach of contract claims. But it can also be drafted more broadly to
include tort claims arising out of whatever subject matter the contract deals
with. Here is an example that incorporates both of those issues:

 XV. Choice of Law

 Florida law governs the interpretation of this Agreement, including
 tort claims arising out of the provision of the Services.

Courts will enforce the law chosen by the parties in the contract unless
either "(a) the chosen state has no substantial relationship to the parties or
the transaction and there is no other reasonable basis for the parties' choice,
or (b) application of the law of the chosen state would be contrary to a
fundamental policy of a state which has a materially greater interest than
the chosen state in the determination of the particular issue and which . . .
would be the state of the applicable law in the absence of an effective
choice of law by the parties." Restatement (Second) of Conflict of Laws §
187 (1971) (emphasis added).

The chosen state has a substantial relationship to the parties or the
transaction when the state is that "where performance by one of the parties

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
is to take place or where one of the parties is domiciled or has his principal
place of business." Id. at comment f. Important considerations in
determining whether a fundamental public policy of the state of the
otherwise applicable law exists are "the extent to which the significant
contacts are grouped in [the] state" and "the extent to which the significant
contacts are grouped in the state of the chosen law." Id.

If the parties to a contract are located in one state, and performance is to
take place in the same state, then choosing another state's law is likely not
an option. But if one or both parties is located in another state, or
performance is to take place in another state, then a choice of law clause
naming that other state's law as the law applicable to the contract may be
enforceable.

As for choice of forum, it wouldn't make sense to draft it as a grant of
rights (the parties already have the right to sue in the applicable forum). It
could be drafted as a condition to an obligation. "If either party sues, they
will do so only in the chosen forum." Or a prohibition: "if either party sues,
they will not do so in any forum other than the chosen forum." But again,
you don't want to sue the party for breach if they bring an action in the
wrong forum. You are describing a choice the parties have made, and you
want the court to respect that choice. So, I would recommend drafting a
choice of forum provision as a statement of policy too. Also, remember to
make clear that the forum is mandatory and exclusive, and applicable only
to litigation, like this:

 XVI. Choice of Forum

 The mandatory and exclusive venue for any litigation arising out of
 this Agreement lies in Jefferson County, Florida.

A majority of courts will enforce a contract clause limiting judicial
jurisdiction, as long as the clause is not unfair or unreasonable. 7 Williston
on Contracts § 15:15 (4th ed.). For example, such a clause would likely not

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
be enforced if it was "obtained by fraud, duress, the abuse of economic
power or other unconscionable means . . . the chosen state would be closed
to the suit or would not handle it effectively or fairly. . . there is good
reason to believe that the plaintiff would be dealt with unfairly in the
chosen state . . . [or] the chosen state would be so seriously an inconvenient
forum that to require the plaintiff to bring suit there would be unjust." Id.

There are multiple additional administrative provisions in contracts, and I
would draft them all in a similar fashion (i.e as descriptive statements).
Here are some examples:

 10. Severability of Agreement

 This Agreement is severable. If any provision is unenforceable, that
 provision is severed, and the remaining provisions continue in effect.

 11. Non-Assignability of Agreement

 This Agreement is not assignable. Any purported assignment of this
 Agreement by either party is invalid.

 12. Binding Nature of Agreement

 This Agreement inures to the benefit of and is binding on all
 permitted successors and assigns.

 13. Headings of Agreement

 The headings in this Agreement are for reference purposes only and
 are not intended to affect the meaning or interpretation of this
 Agreement in any way.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 14. Counterparts of Agreement

 If the parties execute this Agreement in one or more counterparts,
 then all of such counterparts constitute only one Agreement.

 15. Cumulative Nature of Remedies

 All rights and remedies provided in this Agreement are cumulative
 and not exclusive of any other rights or remedies that are to be
 available to the parties.

G. Draft a Testimonium (Closing Statement) Before the Signature
Blocks

After the administrative provisions of a contract comes something called
the "testimonium." (Testimonium is Latin for "witness" or "attestation."
Lewis & Short, Latin Dictionary.) In the old days, the testimonium would
contain lots of legalese. For example, one style was to end the contract
with a statement like this: "Witness our hands and seals this ___ day of
__________, 20__." Today we leave the legalese out and just recite in
plain English that the parties are signing as of the date in the exordium.
That way the document doesn't have multiple different dates, and it is clear
what the correct date is. Here is a sample testimonium:

 Executed by the parties as of the date above.

H. Draft Appropriate Signature Blocks for Each Party

After the testimonium there is just one more thing needed to make the
contract complete and that is a set of signature blocks, one for each party.
What is most important when doing the signature blocks is distinguishing

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
between a person who is signing as an individual and a person who is
signing on behalf of an entity. If the former, the person will be personally
liable for damages resulting from any contract breach. If the latter, the
entity, not the person, will bear that liability.

Here is an example of a signature block for an individual:

 ________________________
 [Name of Signer]

And here is an example of a signature block for an entity (a corporation):

 ______________________
 [Name of Corporation]
 By: [Name of Signer] [Title]

If the party is an entity containing another entity, like a limited partnership
with a corporation as its general partner, you would do the signature block
like this:

 Realty Associates L.P.,
 By its General Partner,

 __________________________
 [Name of Corporation]
 By: [Name of Signer] [Title]

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
To finish a contract, think about how the relationship will end and whether
any funds need to be accounted for, including any money held on deposit.
Then draft appropriate policy provisions. Most of the time they should be
done as descriptive statements. Add a testimonium but leave out the
legalese. And draft appropriate signature blocks, depending upon whether
each party is a person or an entity.
Those are the basic elements of a contract. Now that you have learned the
basics of how to draft and organize contract provisions, you should be able
to draft a simple contract. You may be at a closing and the parties may need
someone to draft an escrow agreement right then and there. You may also
be a litigator handling a case that settles on the courthouse steps, and you
may have to draft the settlement agreement before everyone goes home. In
either case, you should be able to draft a simple contract based on what
you've learned so far.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
                                 Practice Exercises
          Continue drafting the Associate Employment Agreement, Laptop
Purchase Agreement, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of Assets
Agreement and/or Consulting Services Agreement you have been drafting
as the practice exercise for the previous chapter.
        Draft the ending sections of those contracts(s) by determining what
information belongs in the termination section, if applicable,

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
administrative provisions, testimonium and signature lines, and then
drafting each of those provisions.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
7. BRAINSTORMING FOR
FUTURE CONTINGENCIES

T here is more to drafting contracts than transcribing what the client
          tells you and writing it in a clear and organized way. Part of the
          lawyer's job is to document whatever the client says the deal is.
But part of the lawyer's job is also to think into the future about all the
different things that could happen after the agreement is signed; and make
sure the client's interests are protected should any of those contingencies
occur. I call that "brainstorming." The client tells you what the agreement
is, and then you try to think of what additional issues should be addressed,
and what provisions you might add to better protect the client or advance
the client's interests. Also, you sometimes need to do a little research to
make sure whatever you add will be enforced by a court.

A. Address Possible Future Contingencies

Suppose the seller agrees to sell a commercial building to the buyer and
hires you to draft a purchase and sale agreement. Likely the parties talked
about the features of the building, the price and what would have to happen
for the closing to occur. They may have even put together a timeline and
scheduled a date for completion. But they probably did not talk about what
would happen if the building burned to the ground before the closing took
place. That's the type of thing the lawyer needs to think about and address
in the agreement in addition to everything else.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
As another example, suppose two partners agree to start a law firm together
and hire you to draft a partnership agreement. They probably talked about
what their practice would consist of, where their office would be, and how
they would share profits and divide expenses. But they probably didn't
discuss what would happen if at some time in the future one of them died or
decided to leave the partnership. Again, that is an issue the lawyer will
have to raise, resolve between the parties, and then make part of the
agreement. Once you know the terms of the deal, brainstorm for issues that
were left out and contingencies that may happen in the future. Then follow
up with the client, resolve the issues and add the additional language to the
agreement.

B. Examples of Common Issues

There are some contingencies that you will likely want to address in
virtually every contract you draft. For example, if you represent a provider
of goods or services, how can you protect the client from the possibility the
client will have trouble getting paid at some point in the future? What if
you are on the other side, representing a client who may, for innocent
reasons, be late making a payment after the agreement is signed? What can
you do to help both parties to avoid the cost and expense of any future

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
litigation? What if the client gets sued by the other side or some third
party? What can you do to limit the client's exposure to liability? Because
these issues are so common, you should be aware of how they are typically
addressed.

       1) Give Payment Provisions "Teeth"

Most contracts involve an exchange of good or services for money. As a
result, there is usually a risk that, if you represent the payee, your client will
have difficulty getting the other side to pay on time. What can you do then
to make the payment provisions in the agreement more effective?

One thing you can do is impose a late fee if payments are late:
 A. On or before _______ ___, 20__, the Buyer will pay the Seller
 $_____.

 B. If the Buyer pays late, the Buyer will also pay the Seller a late
 fee of $_____.

If that's not enough, have the fee accrue with time, so the later the payment,
the bigger the fee.
 A. On or before _______ ___, 20__, the Buyer will pay the Seller
 $_____.

 B. If the Buyer pays late, the Buyer will also pay the Seller a late
 fee of %____ of the payment amount for every day that the payment
 is late.

When determining the amount of the late fee also keep in mind the purpose
of the fee is to compensate the other party for the untimely performance. If
the purpose or effect is to impose a penalty (e.g. if the amount of the fee is

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
unreasonably large), the requirement to pay the fee may be unenforceable as
against public policy. See Restatement (Second) of Contracts § 356 (1981).

Another thing you can do is draft a clause making clear that timeliness is
material to the agreement, so that lateness is a material breach. This is
typically done by stating that "time is of the essence."
 16. Time of Essence

 Time is of the essence with respect to the Tenant's obligation to pay
 rent in this Agreement.

When you draft a time is of the essence clause be clear what obligation(s)
the clause applies to. See 3A Corbin, Contracts § 715 (1960) ("It is not
desirable to try to achieve this result by merely putting into the contract the
words "time is of the essence of this contract."").

You can also add that if it is necessary to sue to collect amounts unpaid, the
losing party will pay the costs of doing so. But, again, unless you draft the
language to apply only to just the other party, which the other party is
unlikely to agree to, this type of provision also works both ways.
 17. Costs of Collection

 The losing party will pay all reasonable attorneys' fees and costs
 incurred by the prevailing party in enforcing this Agreement.

You should always be thinking not just about the form, but also about the
substance of what you are drafting. You need to make sure what you are
doing accurately represents the terms the parties agreed to. But you should
also be trying to think of ways to better protect your clients' interests. You
should always be looking for opportunities to add value to the deal.

       2) Avoid Disputes Between the Parties

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
If you are on the other side of the transaction, and you represent the person
making the payments, you should consider the possibility that your client
may - for reasons that are not the client's fault - find himself or herself in
breach of the agreement. If that occurs, what can you do to help the client
avoid getting sued? What can you do to give the client the opportunity to
fix the situation, so that the contract is not cancelled, and the relationship
can continue?

One thing you could do is add a grace period to the payment provisions. To
do that, you leave the due date the same, but change the late fee so that it
doesn't start until the end of the grace period, like this:

 C. On or before _______ ___, 20__, the Buyer will pay the Seller
 $_____.

 D. If the Buyer pays more than five (5) days late, the Buyer will
 also pay the Seller a late fee of $_____.

Another way to avoid litigation is for one party to give the other party
notice and an opportunity to cure before commencing litigation.

18. Notice and Opportunity to Cure

Buyer and Seller each agree to provide the other party with written
notice of any default by the other party under this Agreement, and a
period of five (5) business days following the other party's receipt of
such notice to cure such default.

       3) Handle Disputes Outside of Litigation

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Often it is in the interests of both parties to resolve any disputes without
resorting to litigation. This is particularly true for disputes over a relatively
small amount, like the amount of deposits routinely collected in connection
with residential purchase and sale contracts and residential leases. What
can you do in a situation like that to help the parties avoid litigation?

This issue is most commonly addressed by having the parties agree up front
to resolve any disputes through mediation or arbitration. Here is an
example of an obligation to mediate disputes.

 19. Mediation of Disputes

 Before resorting to litigation, the Buyer and Seller will attempt to
 settle any disputes arising out of this Agreement in an amicable
 manner through mediation with a Florida certified or court
 appointed mediator pursuant to the Florida Rules for Certified and
 Court-Appointed Mediators.

If the client wants the mechanism to be binding on the parties, and the
client's concern with avoiding disputes is not so much with the cost of
litigation but rather with the technical nature of the contract and likely
unfamiliarity of any civil judge or jury with the issues dealt with in the
contract, arbitration may be another option. Unlike mediation, arbitration is
binding, and you can arbitrate a dispute with an arbitrator who has expertise
in a specific area. For example, you could draft a provision requiring the
parties to submit disputes to an arbitrator having expertise with technology,
like this:

 20. Arbitration of Disputes

 The parties will submit any controversy or claim arising out of or
 relating to this Contract, or any breach of this Contract, to be
 resolved and determined by binding arbitration administered by a

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 member of the Florida Circuit Civil Mediator Society having case
 experience with technology pursuant to the Florida Arbitration Code,
 §§ 682.01 through 682.25, Fla. Stat.

Other issues which can also be dealt with in a mediation or arbitration
clause include who will pay for the mediator or arbitrator and how that
person will be selected, where and when the mediation or arbitration will
take place, what law will apply, and whether attorney's fees are recoverable.
When you are drafting a mediation provision or an arbitration provision it
is important that you include all essential terms, so that the agreement is
specific enough to be enforced by a court.

       4) Limit the Liability of a Party to the Other

If the client does get sued, how can the client limit its liability to the other
side? If the contract is for the sale of goods, you can use your knowledge of
the Uniform Commercial Code to better protect the client's interests. For
example, Section 2-316(2) of the Code gives a seller the right to exclude or
modify implied warranties:

        [T]o exclude or modify the implied warranty of merchantability or
 any part of it the language must mention merchantability and in case of a
 writing must be conspicuous, and to exclude or modify any implied
 warranty of fitness the exclusion must be by a writing and conspicuous.
 Language to exclude all implied warranties of fitness is sufficient if it
 states, for example, that "There are no warranties which extend beyond
 the description on the face hereof."

As you can see, the exclusion has to be conspicuous. Section 1-201 of the
Code defines conspicuous as follows:

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
    (10)"Conspicuous," with reference to a term, means so written,
 displayed, or presented that a reasonable person against which it is to
 operate ought to have noticed it. Whether a term is "conspicuous" is a
 decision for the court. Conspicuous terms include the following:

 (a)A heading in capitals equal to or greater in size than the
 surrounding text, or in contrasting type, font, or color to the surrounding
 text of the same or lesser size; and
 (b)Language in the body of a record or display in larger type than the
 surrounding text or set off from surrounding text of the same size by
 symbols or other marks that call attention to the language.

The statute also says merchantability must be mentioned. To exclude both
implied warranties, you might draft something like this:

 21. EXCLUSION OF IMPLIED WARRANTIES

 THE IMPLIED WARRANTIES OF MERCHANTIBILITY AND
 FITNESS FOR USE ARE EXCLUDED FROM THIS
 AGREEMENT.

This form would be preferable to using phrases like "as is," and "with all
faults" if the contract contains explicit warranties but excludes implied
warranties.
Section 2-719(3) of the Code gives a seller the right to limit or exclude
consequential damages, as long as the limitation or exclusion is not
"unconscionable":

       Consequential damages may be limited or excluded unless the
limitation or exclusion is unconscionable. Limitation of consequential

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
damages for injury to the person in the case of consumer goods is prima
facie unconscionable but limitation of damages where the loss is
commercial is not.

A contract provision is "unconscionable" if there is "an absence of
meaningful choice on the part of one of the parties together with contract
terms which are unreasonably favorable to the other party." E.g., Bynum v.
Maplebear Inc., 2016 WL 552058 (E.D. N.Y. 2016) (citing text).
Unconscionability may also exist if parties are not represented by counsel,
are unsophisticated or have unequal bargaining power. E.g., Maslowski v.
Prospect Funding Partners LLC, 944 N.W.2d 235 (Minn. 2020). In other
words, an unconscionable contract is a contract of adhesion "drafted and
imposed by a party of superior strength that leaves the subscribing party
little or no opportunity to alter the substantive terms, and only the
opportunity to adhere to the contract or reject it." McKenzie Law Firm, P.A.
v. Ruby Receptionists, Inc., 108 Fed. R. Serv. 3d 312 (D. Or. 2020). To
show that a provision is not unconscionable, it may be appropriate to draft
the provision so that it demonstrates it was "voluntarily" assented to.

For example, if you were adding the exclusion of consequential damages
clause to a contract drafted solely by counsel for one party having superior
bargaining strength over the other unrepresented party, and you wanted to
ensure the enforceability of that clause under those circumstances, you
would likely do more than just make clear the clause only applies to
commercial losses. To attempt to demonstrate the other party had a
meaningful choice to accept or reject the clause, you might consider giving
that other party the option in the contract to either accept the exclusion or
reject it and pay a higher price for the goods. Also, it might make sense to
include in the recitals that the other unrepresented party was a sophisticated
business person who was given ample opportunity to retain counsel prior to
signing the agreement. Here are two an examples of a clause excluding
consequential damages. Both are drafted as descriptive statements, but the
second version is expanded to address issues raised in the caselaw on
unconscionability.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 22. Exclusion of Consequential Damages

 Consequential damages for commercial loss are excluded from this
 Agreement.

 22. Exclusion of Consequential Damages

 Consequential damages for commercial loss are excluded from this
 Agreement, unless the Buyer elects to pay the Seller two (2) times the
 Purchase Price for the Goods by initialing below and making such
 payment.

 Buyer acknowledges that the Buyer is a sophisticated business
 person, familiar with these types of agreements and has had a
 reasonable opportunity to consult with counsel concerning the legal
 effect of this provision.

 ______ Buyer rejects the exclusion of consequential damages, and
 elects that such damages are included. Buyer will pay two (2) times
 the Purchase Price for the Goods.

Section 2-725 of the Code also gives the parties the right to shorten the
statute of limitations for claims arising out of the agreement:

        An action for breach of any contract for sale must be commenced
 within four years after the cause of action has accrued. By the original
 agreement the parties may reduce the period of limitation to not less than
 one year but may not extend it.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
To shorten the statute of limitations, you might draft something like this:

 23. Period of Limitation

        The period of limitation for any action for breach of this
 Agreement is two (2) years after the cause of action accrued.

       5) Protect Against Third Party Claims

The client may also have the risk of being sued by a third party. The classic
case is where the services are provided to a property owner. If someone
gets hurt, the service provider will get sued, and the owner will likely be
sued as well. Under these circumstances, protecting the client from liability
can be achieved by making sure the client will be indemnified for any
losses the client incurs (i.e. the other party to the contract will pay if the
client is sued by a third party). An indemnity provision is an obligation
running from the "indemnitor" to the "indemnitee." Typically, the language
used is "[the indemnitor] will indemnify and hold harmless [the indemnitee]
from and against _________." Also, an indemnification provision should
mention negligence claims and claims seeking attorney's fees, if they are
included; and it should be limited to claims arising out of the applicable
transaction or relationship, so that it is not overbroad. Here is a sample
provision:

 24. Indemnification of Claims

 The Subcontractor will indemnify and hold harmless the Owner, its
 officers, directors, agents, and employees, from and against all third-
 party claims, causes of action, lawsuits and proceedings, including
 claims for negligence, requesting damages, including personal

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 injuries, property damages and economic losses, and reasonable
 attorney's fees, arising out of the lease of the Equipment.

The client is less likely to have to pay a judgment if the person or entity
providing the services has enough money to pay for the loss. As a result, it
is also appropriate to make sure the other side has insurance for the types of
claims that might arise from the performance of services (or any damage to
property or equipment rented). Like an indemnity clause, an insurance
provision is also an obligation. In addition to requiring the party to obtain
insurance, it should require the party to "maintain" the insurance (i.e. not let
the insurance lapse). Also, it is important to mention the type of insurance
required and the policy amounts, as well as add language requiring the party
to add the other side as an additional insured or loss payee and produce
proof of insurance upon request, like this:

 25. Insurance for Equipment

 The Subcontractor will maintain insurance covering liability for
 personal injury in the amount of at least $____________, loss and
 damage to the Equipment for not less than full replacement value of
 the Equipment, which is $__________, and property damage
 insurance in the amount of at least $___________.

 The Subcontractor will name the Owner as a beneficiary or
 additional insured under the applicable policies. The Subcontractor
 will also provide the Owner with copies of certificates evidencing all
 such insurance.

       6) Releasing a Party from Future Claims

You obviously can't draft a provision in a contract releasing a party from
future claims for breach of contract. If you did, the contract would be

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
illusory. But you can draft a clause releasing a party from future negligence
claims, and in many jurisdictions the clause would be enforceable as long as
it is freely and knowingly assented to and the language "clearly,
unambiguously, and unmistakably inform[s] the party relinquishing its
rights of exactly what is being waived." 8 Williston on Contracts § 19:21
(4th ed.). Here is an example of a contractor's release of future negligence
claims against the owner of the property that attempts to inform the signer
of the rights they are relinquishing:

     The Contractor releases the Owner from and against all claims,
 causes of action and lawsuits, including negligence claims, the
 Contract now has now or at any time in the future arising out of the
 construction of the Property. The Contractor assumes the risk of
 accident and injury to the Contractor as a result of providing the
 Services, including the risks of using power tools and other
 construction equipment, and the risks of working alone and with
 others in and around an unfinished building and a partially
 excavated lot.

Those are some examples of the types of provisions you might use to
protect a client from future contingencies. Contract drafting is not just
writing. You need to think of all the things that could happen in the future,
and all the ways a deal could go wrong. Then draft language that will
protect your client should any of those contingencies occur. Brainstorming
is where you really have the opportunity to provide valuable advice and
draft an effective agreement.

Get up to speed first, then draft clear, consistent provisions and organize
them in into a contract. After you do that, brainstorm. Think into the future
about all the different things that could happen after the agreement is signed
and make sure the client's interests are protected should any of those
contingencies occur.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
                                 Practice Exercises
            Review the contract(s) you drafted as the practice exercises for
the previous chapters (the Associate Employment Agreement, Laptop
Purchase Agreement, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of Assets
Agreement or Consulting Services Agreement) and determine whether
there are additional provisions you could add to further or better protect
the client's interests, and then draft the applicable provisions.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
8. DRAFTING WITH
PRECEDENT DOCUMENTS

M ost of the contracts you will be hired to draft have already been
               done before. Whether you are asked to draft a lease, a real
               estate purchase agreement, a promissory note, a mortgage, a
deed, a contract for the sale of goods, a services contract, or an employment
agreement, it's been done before, various examples and forms are widely
available, and there is usually no need to reinvent the wheel. Law students
are prohibited from copying language drafted by someone else, but lawyers
do that all the time. Your law professor is testing you on how well you can
draft on your own. But your client doesn't care if you copied the language
you are using, as long as it works. You can't violate anyone's copyright,
but otherwise you can collect well drafted provisions and documents and
use them to draft whatever type of contracts you need. Still, there is much
more to drafting with precedents than just copying what someone else has
already done.

To be clear, when I say "draft" in this context, what I am referring to is
creating the form of agreement you need from one or more precedents (i.e.
documents someone else wrote). So you are not starting from scratch, like
you were in chapters one through seven. And the precedent is either a form
(it has blanks in in) or a sample (it contains factual information from a prior
transaction).

When you are working with precedents, the drafting process is usually
divided into creating the form you need (the subject of this chapter) and

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
then tailoring the form for the deal you are involved in (the subject of the
next chapter). And there are two ways to create the form you need from
one or more precedents.

The first method for drafting with precedents is to find a sample agreement
that is close to what you are trying to do, and then revise it to create exactly
the document you want. The second method, which applies when you can't
find a sample that is close to what you are trying to do, is to find sample
provisions from documents that are not what you looking for, and use those
sample provisions to piece together the document you want.

I handled a lot of lending transactions in my practice. Whenever I had to
draft something like a promissory note or a mortgage, I had no problem
finding sample documents to use. By using a precedent instead of drafting
from scratch, I was able to save a lot of time, and I could charge the client
less money to get the transaction closed. That is an example of the first
method.

As another example, I also had a client who hired me to draft an option to
purchase air rights to construct residences over commercial real estate in an
urban area. I couldn't find a sample document that was anything close to
what I needed. But I was able to draft an appropriate agreement by looking
at analogous situations and using sample language from a number of
different documents. That is an example of the second method.

Regardless of whether you are able to find a precedent that is close to what
you want or you have to piece a document together using multiple
precedents, there are three steps to the process. Start with a good source for
sample documents. Review any document you find to determine if it is
appropriate for the client and the transaction you are involved in. Revise
the document, if appropriate, to create the form you need; and make sure
the language in the resulting form is consistent throughout.

A. Find a Good Source for Precedent Documents

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
The first step is to find a good source for forms and sample agreements. A
law library is usually a good place to start. Secondary source texts covering
transactional topics like real estate, leasing, lending and finance often have
sample forms. Also, most libraries have continuing legal education
materials containing precedent documents. Often the forms and samples
are in a CD-ROM included with the text, so you can copy the documents
you are interested in without having to re-type each form.
The internet is a good source of sample documents as well. Westlaw has a
service called "Practical Law" that is a good source for form documents.
Westlaw can be costly to use, but there are also plenty of free sites on line.
The Securities and Exchange Commission service called EDGAR
(Electronic Data Gathering, Analysis, and Retrieval) is free a source that
can be used to search for sample business documents. Also, there is a free
website called ONECLE (www.onecle.com) that is a potential source for
sample agreements.

But I would recommend that you stay away from internet sites like
Rocketlawyer and LegalZoom. You can't give a client a document they
could have obtained themselves by visiting a website for consumers. Also,
because sites like that are widely available, as a practical matter, you have
to produce a better document than what those sites sell. No reasonable

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
savvy client is going to pay you to draft a contract if they can get something
just as good by going on line.

Finally, if you work for a law firm, the firm will probably also have a
collection of forms and sample documents you can use. Even if they don't,
the other transactional attorneys you work with will likely have their own
stash of forms and sample documents. So law firms and other lawyers are a
good repository for precedent documents too.

B. Determine if the Precedent is Appropriate for the Client and the
Transaction

Once you have found a precedent to work with the second step is to
determine who it was drafted for (i.e. a seller or a buyer, a lender or a
borrower, a landlord or a tenant, etc.) and under what circumstances (i.e. a
consumer transaction or a commercial transaction, in the state where you
are practicing or somewhere else, for a very large deal or a very small deal,
etc.).

If the document was drafted for a client with interests like those of your
client, and it was intended for a transaction similar to the one you are
involved in, then it may be a good sample to use. If it wasn't then you
should probably keep looking.

For example, you likely wouldn't want to use form language from a lease
written on behalf of a landlord in a contract you are drafting for a tenant.
And it likely wouldn't be appropriate to use provisions from a residential
lease to use for a lease of commercial space.

By the way, this is also the primary reason you should always volunteer to
draft the documents when you represent a client in a transaction. Don't
think you are going to save your client time and money by letting the other
lawyer produce the first draft. It is a lot more efficient to find a document
that reflects your client's interests and fits the circumstances you are dealing
with, and start with that. Revising a document that works for the other side

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
and isn't really appropriate for what you are doing is a much more difficult
and time consuming task.

C. Make Sure the Language in the Document is
Consistent

After you have confirmed the precedent was drafted for a client whose
interests are the same as your client, and it was used in the type of
transaction you are involved in, step three is to decide whether you need or
want to change the style, revise what's there or add anything more. And
then make the changes so that the language is consistent throughout.

If you found a sample that is close to what you were looking for, but the
document uses "shall" for obligations and you want to use "will," then make
that change throughout and make sure you also use "will" in any provisions
you add to the document. If don't want to make that change then leave the
document alone, and make sure you also use "shall" when you add anything
to the document.

If you had to piece the agreement together from precedent language in
different documents, make sure the end result is internally consistent. If
you took a provision from one document that referred to your client as
"Buyer" and another document that used the term "Purchaser," pick one or
the other and use that term consistently throughout the document. In other
words, when you go to revise and finalize the document you have pieced
together, make sure the language in the end result is consistent.

I started this book with a discussion of how to draft from scratch without
using precedent language and documents because you really have to know
that to use precedents well. There is nothing worse than a contract
produced by an attorney that is a collection of provisions drafted in different
styles for different clients in different circumstances, and has never been
revised to make it consistent and appropriate for the transaction it is used
in. When you see documents like that you will know the drafter has no idea
what he is doing. If you do it right, there is more to drafting with precedent
language and documents than just copying someone else's work.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
                                 Practice Exercises
            Research on line sources and find sample agreements and/or
forms documents for the following type(s) of contract(s) you drafted for
the previous practice exercises: Employment Agreement, Purchase
Agreement for Personal Property, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of
Assets Agreement and/or Consulting Agreement.
        Compare the sample documents to the contact(s) you drafted in the
exercises. Are there provisions that are missing from the samples or
sections you could add to the document(s) you drafted from scratch? Are
common sections drafted similarly or differently? What are the pros and
cons using the language of the different provisions instead of the language
you drafted?

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
9. TAILORING PRECEDENT
DOCUMENTS FOR A DEAL

W hen you are practicing as a transactional lawyer and
                representing the same client in multiple similar transactions,
                like doing the leases for a client who manages shopping malls,
you will already have the forms you need most of the time, so you won't
have to go looking for precedents. Instead, your job will be to tailor the
forms you have for use in specific deals. So that is the subject of this
chapter: tailoring form documents for a deal (i.e. adding to the document
the factual information for the transaction and making appropriate revisions
