for the transaction).

For example, if you're working at a firm in the commercial lending
department, you'll probably be working with a set of complex form
documents the bank uses for all its commercial loans, or the firm uses
whenever it represents a bank in a commercial loan. You won't have to
draft the documents from scratch. Instead, you'll need to tailor the forms to
reflect the facts of a particular deal, and then close the transaction. And
there is more too that than just filling in the blanks in the documents.

Working with complex form documents is very different than drafting
agreements from scratch. If you aren't familiar with the form, you need to
study it first so you understand what it says and how it was put together.
Draft the revisions the same way the rest of the document is drafted. Then
read the document again to see if any other provisions are affected by the
changes you made. And keep track of all the revised drafts you create.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
A. Read the Form to Familiarize Yourself with the
Substance and Structure

A lengthy business contract can be intimidating if you are not familiar with
it. You may not know the subject matter; and the terminology may be alien
to you. The type may be small and the paragraphs densely packed. Most
people avoid reading contracts like that. But someone has to read them to
figure out what they say. And that someone is you. So just take your time
and go through the document section by section. If there are terms you
don't recognize, look them up. If the applicable law is something you are
not familiar with, you may have to do a little research. Take breaks if you
lose your concentration. The ability to scour a lengthy business document
is a skill you will develop with time.

Depending upon the circumstances, you will likely want to take notes as
you read; and it may help you to create an outline of the contract's terms.
The situation is analogous to reading a long, complex statute for the first
time. You really need to study the language to fully understand how the
document works and how it was put together. You can't revise one section
and then be oblivious to what is contained in the rest of the form. So do
what you have to do to familiarize yourself with the document before you
start revising it.

B. Draft any Revisions the Same Way the Document was Drafted

When you are ready to start filling in the blanks and making revisions, there
are three rules I would recommend you follow. First, draft the revisions the
same way the rest of the document was drafted, even if it was drafted
differently than what you learned in the first few chapters of this book. In
other words, make whatever changes you have fit in with the rest of the
document.

If you are adding a background provision, and the form starts each
background provision with the word "whereas," then you should start your
background provision the same way, even though "whereas" is legalese. If

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
the form uses "shall" for obligations, you should use "shall" for obligations,
even if you agree with me that "will" is plainer English than "shall." Also,
use the document's definitions in the provisions you draft.

As a practical matter, you can't revamp every document from beginning to
end to conform to what you learned in this book, and then send the client a
bill for the time you spent doing something you weren't asked to do. You
still need to be concerned with clarity, completion, and accuracy; but you
also need to follow the same rules the original drafter followed when they
drafted the document. Everyone drafts a little differently; when you are
working on someone else's documents, you need to respect the decisions
the drafter made and the style they felt was appropriate. So draft the
revisions the same way the document was drafted, even if it was drafted
differently than what you learned so far.

C. Read Closely the Rest of the Document to See if Other Provisions are
Affected

After you have revised a provision in a form document, read closely the
rest of the provisions to see if they are affected. If they are, either confirm
the provisions still work as intended with the new language or revise the
provisions as well. The documents used in large commercial deals are often
complicated; you have to appreciate that complexity and make sure your
revisions don't screw up the rest of the form. So closely read the rest of the
document whenever you add to, delete, or change the language that is
already there.

If the document is set up for a corporation, for example, you are filling in
the blanks, and it turns out the borrower is a sole proprietorship, then check
to see if there are other provisions of the document that have to be changed
as well. For example, the representation and warranty that "the Borrower is
a duly organized and existing Florida corporation" will need to be revised.
The obligation that "the Borrower will file annual reports with the Secretary
of State" will have to be revised. And the prohibition that "the Borrower
will not amend its articles of organization without the Lender's consent"

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
will also need to be changed. None of that language applies if you change
the borrower from a corporation to a sole proprietorship.

Don't rely only on the "search" command in the word processing software
you use. If you search for "Massachussetts" that will help you find the
references to that word in the document. But if there are places where the
document uses the abbreviation "Mass." you will miss them if you just
search for "Massachusetts." If the word is misspelled somewhere in the
document, you will miss the misspelled word. Also, if there are references
to the "Commonwealth" that need to be changed to the "State" (e.g. if you
ware changing the form to refer to a state instead of a commonwealth), you
will miss those as well.

Any time you delete a defined term you will need to search through the
document for every time that term was used and (1) confirm deleting the
term is appropriate (it may have been used for something you didn't
anticipate); (2) delete the term; and (3) make sure the remaining language
still works properly (e.g. if the only reason for the provision is to say
something about the term, then you would delete the whole thing).

When you delete an entire subsection in an agreement, realize that doing
that will throw off the numbering of subsequent subsections. When you do
that, either replace the deleted language with the statement "intentionally
omitted" or renumber the entire section.

Also, if you renumber the entire section, make sure references to the
renumbered paragraphs elsewhere in the document are also changed. You
might have a provision that says a party can exercise its rights in "paragraph
seven." If you deleted a paragraph, and paragraph seven is now paragraph
six, you need to change the reference to paragraph seven as well.

Needless to say, it is difficult to pour through a long document over and
over, every time you do a new deal, to make sure the substance matches
what the client actually agreed to do, all the factual information is correct,
and the language in the document is internally consistent. To make it easier,
some practitioners print out the document and read it with pen in hand,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
rather than on a computer screen. Others read the contract slowly out loud
to help them spot issues. I find it helps to look at one subject at a time, so I
check all the paragraph numbering in one pass through, double check all the
dates and dollar amounts in another pass through, check for name and
address spelling, and then check for proper usage of defined terms.
Another good habit to get into is going back to the notes of your original
client conference to make sure you addressed all the issues (that's also a
good practice for completing a final exam in a contract drafting class).
Complex documents tend to involve high dollar amounts. When a client
entrusts you with handling a large transaction, there is really no such thing
as being too careful.

D. Example of How to Revise a Complex Form Document

Let me give you an example to further demonstrate and explain what is
involved in tailoring a complex agreement form for a specific type of deal.
Take a look at the Construction Loan Agreement in the Appendix to this
book. Let's walk through what is involved in making two simple changes
to that document. The first change is to delete the "Commercial Lease."
The form is written for the development of a property that has been leased
to the borrower under a long term commercial lease. To make the form
work for a deal in which the borrower actually owns the property, we will
need to delete the Commercial Lease from the form. Also, the form is
drafted for a project in Massachusetts involving borrowers and guarantors
organized under Massachusetts law. The second change is to make the
document work for a project in Florida involving borrowers and guarantors
organized under Florida law. What is involved in making those two simple
changes to this complex agreement?

The first step is to find any reference to the "Commercial Lease" in the
agreement. As it turns out, that term only appears once, in the definition of
a "Leasehold Interest" in Section 1.3 of the Agreement:

 "Leasehold Interest" means the leasehold interest of ____________in that
 portion of the Mortgaged Premises leased to ____________pursuant to
 the terms of that certain Commercial Lease, dated _____________,

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 between ____________and ________________, as amended by that
 certain Amendment to Lease dated ____________, and by that certain
 Restated Amendment to Lease dated effective as of ______________.

Of course, if you delete the reference to a Commercial Lease, then there is
no longer any Leasehold Interest. So if you delete the Commercial Lease,
then you also need to delete the definition of a Leasehold Interest. And
then, since the agreement no longer involves any Leasehold Interest, you
also have to search through the document to find provisions referring to that
term and modify them as appropriate.

If you start at the beginning of the document, the first time you will find the
term Leasehold Interest used is in the list of instruments securing the loan in
Section 1.6 where the "Leasehold Mortgage" is referred to.

 (b) The Leasehold Mortgage, Security Agreement and Assignment of
 Leases and Rents (the "Leasehold Mortgage"), from the Borrower to the
 Bank dated of even date herewith, encumbering ____________'s
 Leasehold Interest, and the personal property located thereon and used in
 connection therewith;

Of course, if you delete the reference to the Leasehold Interest, then there is
no longer any Leasehold Mortgage, so this paragraph referring to and
defining the Leasehold Mortgage also needs be deleted in its entirety. And
since it is numbered, the deleted language should be referred to as
"intentionally omitted," like this:

 (b) [Intentionally omitted.]

Having deleted these two terms from the agreement, the next step would be
to search the document for references to either or both of them, and modify
the applicable provisions as appropriate. For example, at the end of Section
1.6 there is a reference to the Loan Documents as including, among other
things, the Leasehold Mortgage. That reference should be deleted.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 This Agreement, the Note, the Fee Mortgage, the Leasehold Mortgage,
 the Assignment of Leases, the Assignment of Permits, the five Site
 Professional Consents, the Guaranties, the Financing Statements, the
 Environmental Indemnity Agreement, the Security Agreement and
 Assignment of Earnout Advance, the Blocked Account Agreement, and
 all other documents, instruments and agreements relating to and executed
 in connection with the Loan, as the same may be amended, restated,
 replaced, supplemented, extended, substituted, consolidated or otherwise
 modified from time to time, are collectively referred to as the "Loan
 Documents."

Similarly, references to the Leasehold Mortgage in Sections 3(g), 6.1(c), 10
and 19 should be deleted. And reference to the Leasehold Interest in
Section 6.1(m) should be deleted as well.

The second step is to find and revise any references to the project being in
Massachusetts and involving borrowers and guarantors organized under
Massachusetts law. And that step is a little more difficult. Start by revising
Massachusetts to Florida in the preamble:

 THIS CONSTRUCTION LOAN AGREEMENT (this "Agreement") is
 dated as of the ____ day of ___________, by and among
 _________________________, a Massachusetts Florida limited liability
 company ("________"), and _____________________________, a
 Massachusetts Florida limited liability company ("___________"), both
 having a usual place of business at _______________________,
 ___________, ______________ _____ (collectively with their respective
 successors and assigns, the "Borrower"), ____________, in his individual
 capacity, _____________________________________, a Massachusetts
 Florida limited liability company and _________, a Massachusetts
 Florida limited liability company all having an address at
 _____________________________, __________, ______ _____
 (collectively with their respective successors and assigns, the individual
 guarantor is hereinafter referred to as the "Individual Guarantor" and the
 remaining guarantors, the "LLC Guarantors"; together, the Individual
 Guarantor and the LLC Guarantors are hereinafter referred to collectively

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 as the "Guarantors") and _______________________, having a usual
 place of business at ____________________, ________, _____________
 _____ (together with its successors and/or assigns, the "Bank").

Make the same substitution in Sections 5(o) and 6.1(r).

That is easy enough but in Sections like 3(a), you need to go a little further.
References to the "Commonwealth of Massachusetts" need to be changed
to the "State of Florida," since Massachusetts is a commonwealth and
Florida is a state.

 (a) Each Borrower is a limited liability company duly organized and
     validly existing under the laws of the Commonwealth of
     Massachusetts State of Florida. Each of the LLC Guarantors is a
     limited liability company duly organized and validly existing under
     the laws of the Commonwealth of Massachusetts State of Florida

Sections 6.1(u) and 20need to be revised similarly.

But then when you get to a section like 5(q) you need to go even further.

 (q) Borrower agrees not to suffer any attachment (except for Notices
 of Contract under Mass. Gen. Laws Chapter 254 or Statements of
 Account thereunder, provided that (i) with respect to any such Notices of
 Contract or Statements of Account, the Borrower informs the Bank of the
 filing of the same upon Borrower's acquiring knowledge of same and (ii)
 with respect to any Statements of Account only, the Borrower bonds over
 such lien, provides an endorsement to the Title Policy issued by the Title
 Company insuring the priority of the Mortgage over such lien, or
 otherwise addresses the lien established thereby to the reasonable
 satisfaction of the Bank prior to the earlier of (a) twenty (20) days of
 Borrower's acquiring knowledge of the same, and (b) the date on which
 the lien attains priority over the lien of the Mortgage), whether by trustee
 process or otherwise, to be made against Borrower's interest in this
 Agreement or any payment hereunder. In addition, the Borrower further

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 agrees not to assign or transfer this Agreement or any interest herein or
 right to payment hereunder (except for a Permitted Transfer as defined
 below) without the prior written consent of Bank, which Bank may give
 or withhold in its absolute discretion. Any such assignment or transfer
 made without Bank's consent shall be void and of no force or effect and
 shall at Bank's election be an immediate Event of Default hereunder.

The parenthetical after the reference to attachments refers to "Notices of
Contract" under Massachusetts statute Mass. Gen. Laws Chapter 254. To
revise that section, you need to do a little research. If Florida has a similar
statute, then revise the language to track the statute and provide the
appropriate citation to Florida statutes. If it doesn't, then it may be
appropriate to either delete the parenthetical entirely or revise it in some
other way to protect the client's interests.

Section 6.1(v) involves a similar issue:

 (v) For the LLC Guarantors, (i) a certified copy of the Certificate of
 Organization for each LLC Guarantor issued by the Secretary of the
 Commonwealth of Massachusetts Florida, dated no more than thirty (30)
 days prior to the date hereof, and (ii) a Certificate of Legal Existence and
 Good Standing for each LLC Guarantor issued by the Secretary of the
 Commonwealth of Massachusetts Florida dated no more than thirty (30)
 days prior to the date hereof, and (iii) a Member's Certificate issued by
 the sole Member of each LLC Guarantor indicating the due authorization
 of the transactions contemplated by this Agreement.

Again, you need to do a little research. Are Certificates of Organization
and Certificates of Legal Existence issued by the Secretary of the state of
Florida or some other government official? Also, are they properly referred
to in Florida as Certificates of Organization and Certificates of Legal
Existence or is some other terminology used in Florida? To revise this
section, you have to find the answers to those questions first, and then you
will be able to change the language as appropriate.

These issues also arise in Section 20.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 20. GOVERNING LAW. Each of the parties hereto agrees that this
 Agreement and all the Loan Documents were executed in Massachusetts
 Florida, and are governed by and construed according to the laws of The
 Commonwealth of Massachusetts State of Florida. Borrower hereby
 consents to the personal jurisdiction of The Commonwealth of
 Massachusetts State of Florida in connection with any action arising out
 of or in connection with the Loan, this Agreement and/or the other Loan
 Documents. The Bank's obligations under this Agreement and its
 obligations to make any advance or to make any future commitment is
 subject to Statutory Loan Limitations as set forth in Massachusetts
 General Laws Chapter 167E, Section 14. Consistent with said Statutory
 Loan Limitations, the Bank may refuse to fund the Loan or any advance,
 may require a principal reduction in an existing balance, and may sell
 participations, in any instance where a loan limit violation could occur.

As you can see, just making those two changes cannot be accomplished
with two quick edits, and it is not as simple as just replacing those two
terms throughout the document. Every time you change something in a
complex form document like this you have to read closely the rest of the
document to see if other provisions are affected, and then modify those
provisions as appropriate.

D. Keep Track of Any Changes You Make

After you are done revising the document you are using, make sure to keep
track of the changes you made. Always start with a copy of the original
document and keep the original unedited document in your files. The
changes you make for one deal may not be appropriate to use in another
deal you work on in the future. If they aren't, it may make a lot more sense
to go back to the original document and change that, than change the
revised version again.

Once you have a set of documents drafted, and before you send them to the
other side, send a set of drafts to your client to review. Make sure the
changes are highlighted and the client can see what you did, especially the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
factual information you entered, and confirm it is accurate. You may have
misheard something the client said, or the client may have misspoken.
Regardless of the reason, it is important to fix any mistakes before the
documents go out the door.

Some clients will just skim what you send them, and then send it back with
a thank you note (in fact some clients may think of the drafting as your job
and not understand why you are seeking their input). Other clients will go
through your edits with a fine-tooth comb and give you a lot of feedback.
In my opinion the latter type is far preferable to work with. Just don't get
defensive if you get a lot of comments. Better to incorporate those
suggestions and make the agreement perfectly match what the client wants,
than to go without them and have a document signed that doesn't accurately
conform to the deal.

Once all the proofreading is done, provide the documents to the attorney on
the other side of the transaction. In a commercial transaction, the attorney
representing the other side will likely have additional proposed revisions.
You will have responses to those revisions. And there may be other parties
in the transaction who also want to revise the documents. As a result, it is
important to keep track of all these changes, who made them, and when
they were made. In the old days, that was done by keeping paper copies of
each set of revisions in manila files. Today, it is more common to just keep
copies of the computer files, labeled appropriately. For example, a file
containing the borrower's first set of revisions to a loan agreement might
have a filename like this: "loanagre.rev1.borr.2.28.2019.doc"

As for the proposed changes, they are usually done by marking up the
original document. When paper was popular, an attorney would handwrite
comments on a "hard copy" of the agreement and send it to the other side
by "fax." Today, it is more common to use the "track changes" feature in a
program like Word (You could also use comment bubbles or even
footnotes), and then send the comments to the other side by e-mail.

If you are the original producer of the document at issue, you have an
interest in making sure all the requested revisions work together, and the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
document is still clear and coherent after it is revised. Sometimes when
there are too many "cooks in the kitchen," the revisions can get out of hand,
and the document suffers. If you are the original drafter, it is part of your
job not to let that happen. After all the changes are in, you will likely have
to go back through the document to make everything hang together more
uniformly, and then circulate a revised draft.

Once you have the document in final form, save it in some way that it
cannot be changed. If you are handling a closing, the other parties are
relying on you to produce the final version of the document at the closing.
As a practical matter there isn't enough time for every attorney to proofread
every page of every document while everyone else is at the closing waiting
to sign. So, everyone has to rely on you. And if you let them down by
unintentionally substituting the wrong draft, your reputation will be
irreparably harmed. So always keep careful track of which document is the
final version!

Remember, completing a form document for a deal is not just an exercise in
filling in the blanks. If it was, the client wouldn't be asking you to do it.
Your job is to tailor the document to the deal. That involves closely reading
the document and revising it with an appreciation of its complexity and how
it was originally put together. So, use your brain, and think about whether
the facts fit the substantive provisions. If they don't, make appropriate
revisions to the language. And make the changes fit by following the same
protocols the original drafter followed. That is how you complete a set of
form documents for a deal.

                                   Practice Exercise

         Complete the Construction Loan Agreement in the Appendix of
 this book for the following deal:

         The borrower is an individual named Alberto Salvatori. He lives
 at 333 South Street in Micanopy, Florida. The lender is Archer Bank,
 which has a branch office in Micanopy at 4 Sunrise Terrace. There are no
 guarantors.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
        The project will be a single-family home in Macintosh, Florida.
The address is 7 Palm Court, Macintosh. The borrower plans to occupy
the property after the closing.

        The loan is for $150,000. There will be no retainage.
        There are no junior lenders.
        The lender approved Homes, Inc. as the contractor for the project.
There is no architect. When the project is done, the contractor will certify
compliance with applicable laws.
        The borrower will be using part of the loan to acquire the property.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
10. REVISING THE OTHER
SIDE'S COMPLETED
DOCUMENTS

T he other situation you are often involved if you have a
          transactional practice is proposing revisions to a completed
          document when you represent the other side in a deal (i.e. you are
not the one who created the form or tailored the document for the deal).
That usually involves qualifying the contract language, imposing
conditions, and narrowing the scope of the original provisions. If your
client is asked to sign a document you didn't draft, chances are very good
you will need to go through this aspect of dealing with complex
documents. And that is true even if the other side claims they are using a
"standard" form and presents it to you like it's written in stone. (The one
exception to this rule I am aware of is for the loan documents used by
Fannie Mae. Those loans are securitized and sold in the secondary market.
For that and other reasons, there is really nothing you can do to revise the
language on behalf of a borrower. It is still important to explain to the
borrower what the substance of those documents is. But this discussion on
revising a document to protect a client's interests really doesn't apply in that
context.)

A. Qualify the Language in the Agreement

The primary way you protect the client's interests in documents you have
not drafted yourself is by qualifying the language in the documents. For

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
example, suppose there is an obligation in the document that says this:
 The Manager will remove all lead paint from the interior of the
 Premises.

If you represent the owner of the premises, you could make that obligation
stronger by changing it to this:
 The Manager will remove all lead paint from the interior of the
 Premises to the satisfaction of the Owner.

If the owner drafted the language and you represented the manager, you
could make the language weaker by revising it to this:
 The Manager will use reasonable efforts to remove all lead paint from
 the interior of the Premises.

And if both parties were negotiating the language, they might agree on an
objective standard that is somewhere in between those two extremes by
revising it to say this:
 The Manager will remove all lead paint from the interior of the
 Premises in a good and workmanlike manner.

Depending on who you represent, you can qualify the language to better
serve your client's interests. By qualifying the language, you are changing
the level of risk that each party has to bear. The first example puts all the
risk on the manager. In the second example, the owner is taking more risk.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Another example might be the language used in representations and
warranties. A seller in a real estate transaction might not be comfortable
representing that "there are no hazardous wastes on the Property." The
seller would know whether or not the seller dumped any hazardous wastes
on the property. But the seller would have no way of knowing what
happened before the seller owned the property. So, to better protect the
seller's interests, the representation could be modified to say, "to the best of
the Seller's knowledge, there are no hazardous wastes on the Property." By
making that qualification, you transfer some of the risk from the seller to
the buyer.

B. Impose Conditions on Rights, Obligations and Prohibitions

You can also change the allocation of risk in a document by imposing
conditions on the exercise of rights by the other side, the imposition of
obligations on your client, and the applicability of prohibitions that affect
your client. Suppose, for example, an employer has the right to test an
employee's blood for the presence of illegal drugs. The applicable
provision might simply say this:

 The Employer may obtain and test a sample of the Employee's blood
 to look for the presence of illegal drugs; and the Employee will
 submit to such tests when requested.

You could limit the employer's rights by imposing conditions on the
language, like this:

 After first giving the Employee written notice at least thirty (30) days
 before the proposed test date, the Employer may obtain and test a
 sample of the Employee's blood to look for the presence of illegal
 drugs; and the Employee will submit to such tests, unless the
 Employee has a valid medical reason for refusing to do so.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
By imposing these conditions, you transfer some of the risk from the
employee to the employer.

C. Narrow the Scope of Language in Rights, Obligations and
Prohibitions

Yet another option is to narrow the scope of the language whenever the
other side is given a right or your client is subject to an obligation or
prohibition. This is particularly helpful when dealing with exculpatory
provisions, which are often drafted in an unreasonably broad way. For
example, here is a release from an apartment lease:

 The Tenant releases, remises and forever discharges the Landlord,
 and the Landlord's employees and agents, from any claims, causes of
 action or lawsuits the Tenant has now or may have in the future.

Ideally, you would just delete that provision, if you represent a tenant. But
at the very least you can revise it to make the language narrower, like this:

 The Tenant releases, remises and forever discharges the Landlord
 from any claims, causes of action or lawsuits arising out of the use of
 the Property the Tenant has now and is currently aware of.

When you are proposing changes like this, you should do the changes in a
manner that shows the other attorney what you're proposing to revise; and
makes it easy for the other attorney to accept your changes, if they are
agreeable to making them. Don't just ask the attorney to revise a provision
to address an issue you are concerned about. Make the revisions for her, so
that they can just accept the change if they are agreeable. For example, if I

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
were requesting a revision to the release provision above, I would do it like
this:

 The Tenant releases, remises and forever discharges the Landlord, and the
 Landlord's employees and agents, from any claims, causes of action or
 lawsuits arising out of the use of the Property the Tenant has now or may
 have in the future and is currently aware of.

D. Change the Numbers; Ask to Receive More and Pay Less

Another way you can advance your client's interests in a document is to
look for the numbers in the agreement, and determine whether it would be
in your client's interest to propose changes in the amounts. But distinguish
between the core consideration for the deal, which the client has already
agreed to, and all the other numbers that the other party's lawyer added
when he drafted the contract you are seeking to revise.

For example, if the client agreed to pay $1,000,000 for the property, that
amount is probably not something you can revise. But if the attorney added
a late fee of 12% per annum to any late payments, and that issue had not
previously been discussed, then that is definitely something you should
negotiate on your client's behalf.

If the agreement provides for other payments to be made by your client,
then it is obviously in your client's interest to pay less. If you client is the
one receiving the payments, then the opposite is true. If payments are to be
made within some time period, then more time is better if you client is the
one making the payments. Less time is better if your client is the one
receiving the payments. If there is an interest rate applicable to past due
amounts, the situation is analogous. Negotiate for a lower rate if your client
will be obligated to pay it, and a higher rate if your client will be receiving
the payments.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Also, if your client is agreeing to pay an undetermined amount, like
expenses incurred by the other party, define those expenses narrowly to
limit that liability. Also consider adding a cap or limit on what your client
is agreeing to pay.

Those are just a few examples of the types of dollar amounts, time periods
and other numbers you may find in an agreement and can revise to better
protect your client's interests. Put yourself in the client's shoes and imagine
you are the one who is being requested to sign what the other lawyer
drafted. If you wouldn't sign because the numbers are excessive or unfair,
then raise those issues on your client's behalf.

E. Don't Think Industry Forms Are Written in Stone

There are times when documents are presented in a format that makes it
look like they cannot be edited, but the reality is there is no reason why you
can't change them. Examples of those types of documents are the listing
agreement forms and real estate purchase and sale agreement forms that are
drafted by trade associations in the real estate industry for use in residential
real estate transactions. Those forms may be popular but there is nothing
"standard" about them; they were drafted for the people the trade
represents. Brokers represent sellers so most realtor generated forms are
drafted primarily to represent sellers. If you represent a buyer, there will
likely be some changes you will want to make.

So, if you get something like a listing agreement for a residential property
in a computerized format that cannot easily be revised, one option you have
is to print the document out and then hand write or type the edits on the
hard copy before the document is signed. That would be very "old
school." If the signing is being done online, another option is to add the
changes as an addendum to the document, make sure the document
incorporates the addendum by reference, and have the parties also sign the
addendum. Here is an example:

                   Addendum to Limited Listing Agreement

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Concerning 123 Black Acre, Alachua, FL

Notwithstanding anything to the contrary in the Listing Agreement
(the Agreement) executed on _________ __, 20__ by and between
Realty Corp. (the Broker) and Bob and Mary Smith (the Sellers)
concerning the property located at 123 Black Acre, Alachua, Florida
(the Property), the parties agree as follows:

The sales commission referred to in the Agreement will be due and
payable if, as and when the sale of the Property to the Buyers closes
and the deed to the Property is transferred to the Buyers, and not
otherwise.

Executed as of this __ day of ___________, 20__.

___________________     ____________________
Bob Smith, Seller     Realty Corp.

____________________           By: [Name][Title]
Mary Smith, Seller

When the attorney representing the other side is responsible for drafting the
documents, your job is not to just read through what the other attorney
drafted and tell the client it is o.k. to sign. Your job is to participate in the
drafting process by revising the documents to protect and/or advance your
client's interests. Qualify the language, impose conditions, and limit the
scope of provisions as appropriate. Follow the protocols in the agreement;
and make it easy for the other side to see what you are asking for and make
the requested change. If there is a disagreement over the language, then
talk to the other lawyer and work it out. I have some advice on contract
negotiations in the next chapter.

                                  Practice Exercises

Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
        Revise the Construction Loan Agreement in the Appendix of this
Handbook to better advance the borrower's interests in the transaction.
For example,

         Qualify appropriate representations "to the best of the Borrower's
knowledge."

        Obligate the lender to be reasonable in its requirements and the
borrower to do work reasonably satisfactory to the Lender.

         Consent should not be unreasonably withheld, conditioned or
delayed, and costs should also be reasonable.

        Revise overbroad language to make it narrower.
         Appropriately condition the exercise of rights affecting the
borrower.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
11. NEGOTIATING
CONTRACT PROVISIONS

N egotiating contract provisions is very different than negotiating
            something like the settlement of a case. For one thing, there are
            no dollar amounts to deal with. Also, the process is less
adversarial. In litigation, if the settlement falls through, you just go back to
fighting it out in court. If you are trying to document a transaction and the
negotiations fall through, the transaction doesn't happen. That not only
means both sides are upset at you and the other lawyer; it also likely means
neither of you get paid. Clients like deal makers, not deal killers. Plus,
transactional lawyers often collect their fee from the proceeds of the
transaction. So, you never want to let the deal fall through because you and

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
the other lawyer couldn't figure out a reasonable way to document the
terms. You need to be a good negotiator. Do what you need to get prepared
before you start negotiating. Adopt a strategy for getting what you really
want. And then resolve the issues and get the agreement done.

A. Meet with the Client

Before you start the negotiating make sure you are prepared. Meet with the
client before you meet with the other attorney and find out two things.
First, go through the terms of the deal and find out what is important to the
client. What are the items the client must have for the deal to proceed; and
what are the things the client can do without? In other words, what terms
are not negotiable and what terms are flexible? As to the latter, what
options are there? Is there a backup position the client could live with?
What other options do you have if you can't get the client's first choice?

Second, find out how much bargaining power the client has. Who needs
this deal more: your client or the other side? Who has a more substantial
interest in the deal? If the deal is a finance transaction and your client is
lending much less money than the other banks, then your client likely has
much less leverage. What is the market like for the goods or services your
client is providing? Is it a buyer's market or a seller's market? If your client
is an employee and you are negotiating an employment agreement, is your
client very valuable to the firm? Or is he lucky he got the job?

Meet with your client and at least find out those two things before you start
negotiating. Don't make the mistake of accomplishing something your
client really doesn't need, while failing to get something your client must
have. And don't go into a negotiation making a lot of demands and
embarrass yourself by having to give in on everything because your client
has no bargaining power. Iron those issues out before the process begins.

B. Develop a Strategy

After you have met with the client, take the time to come up with a strategy
for the negotiations. Have a plan for how you want to proceed. Do you

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
want to put some "throwaways" on the table, and then try to get what you
want by giving up the "throwaways" in exchange for what you really want?
Do you want to get the client involved and play "god cop; bad cop" with
one of you as the good cop and the other as the bad cop? How about testing
the water with some relatively unimportant provisions to find out how the
other side negotiates, then move on to more important provisions? He may
be a haggler, someone who likes to cut to the chase or someone who is
obstinate in negotiations and refuses to change anything. Better to know
which before you get to the important issues.

Also, make sure you have an explanation for each change you are
requesting and a good argument for why it should be made. It may not be
enough to just mark up a document, send it to the other side, and wait for a
response. The other attorney may want to go through each revision and
understand why you are requesting it. Regardless you will also want to be
ready with one or more back up requests if you can't get your first choice.
As an example, you might want to change the language to say the work will
be done "to the satisfaction of" your client. But, as a backup, you might
also be willing to agree to having the work done to meet certain specific
performance standards.

And find out who you will be dealing with before you start. Google the
lawyer on the other side and see what their background is. Maybe ask some
associates who might know them what their style is. And do the same thing
for the client on the other side. Anything you can find out about the parties
could be helpful to you in negotiations. If you are going to deal with a
bully, it is better to know that going in. If the lawyer has a reputation for
being difficult but the client is a reasonable business person, suggest the
lawyers and the clients meet together. Any information you can get about
the participants may be helpful to ensure the negotiations are productive.

C. Negotiate with the Other Side

Once you are ready, try to do the negotiations on your own terms. Ideally,
you would have the other attorney come to your office to talk about the
revisions. But that may not be practicable. Likely, the other attorney wants

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
you to come to their office. They don't want to drive all the way to the
other side of town, and neither do you. Plus, both sides want to get through
the process as efficiently as possible, so most negotiations end up
happening over the phone.

If the negotiations are going to happen by telephone, make sure you are the
one making the call. If the other attorney calls and catches you off guard,
tell him you are busy with another project and will call back. Don't deal
with the call if you aren't ready. Don't let the other side dictate when the
negotiations happen.

Also, once the negotiations start, in addition to making your own
presentation, listen carefully to how the other side has to say. If you can
figure out where they have flexibility and where their hands are tied you
will be better able to advance your client's interests, and you will be more
successful at resolving any impasses you encounter. Try to figure out who
the decision maker is on the other side. Is the attorney driving the
negotiations or just doing what the client tells him to do? Are the two of
them working together or is one of them letting the other one deal with
whatever it is you are proposing? If the negotiations are in person, watch
closely for the other person's body language, micro expressions and
reactions to the issues you raise during the negotiations. What you hear and
see is at least as important as what you have to say.

Most importantly, never be a deal killer. Always be a deal maker. In
complex transactions there are often multiple attorneys revising a
document. Everyone has to advance their client's interests, but everyone
also has to work together to get the deal done. You want to be assertive and
persuasive about the changes you request. You don't want to meekly
submit some comments and then not even follow up to find out if any were
acceptable to the other side. But you never want to get the reputation for
being a deal killer. Everyone has to get along for the deal to happen. So,
when you are advocating for a particular revision, be sensitive to the other
interests involved and look for ways to compromise.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Conduct your negotiations with the mindset that you are part of a team of
professionals in charge of making the deal happen. There is seldom any
harm done by opening your mouth and asking for a change in the language.
If you represent a small player in a big project, you may need to choose
your battles more carefully than you would otherwise. But you still need to
say something, if for no other reason than to justify your existence. No one
expects you to be a wallflower. Lawyers are not wallflowers. But you are
also not a litigator concerned only with advocating your client's position.
You need to be assertive, but you also need to compromise. You need to be
an advocate, but you also need to be a deal maker, not a deal killer. If you
are good at doing that, your services will always be in demand.

                                  Practice Exercises
         Pretend you represent the other side in the transaction involved in
 the Associate Employment Agreement, Laptop Purchase Agreement,
 Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of Assets Agreement and/or
 Consulting Services Agreement you drafted as the practice exercises for
 the previous chapters. Draft proposed revisions to the contract(s) you
 drafted, then give the original document to another student in your class,
 ask the student to represent the side you originally represented, and
 negotiate your proposed revisions with the student.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
12. ETHICAL ISSUES IN
CONTRACT DRAFTING

E thical issues are not easy to deal with in a transactional practice.
          Most of the ethical rules are for litigators not transactional
          attorneys, so there is often not much guidance. But there are still
plenty of ethical pitfalls for transactional attorneys. And they are even
more difficult to deal with when you are just starting out. If you commit
malpractice you may lose your job, but professional liability insurance will
likely cover the loss, assuming you have it. If you commit a violation of the
rules of ethics, you could lose your license. So, the stakes are high!

Suppose you are working in a law firm, helping a senior partner close a
loan. You send a draft loan agreement to borrower's counsel. Unknown to
you, the document contains metadata showing the deletion of an "ipso
facto" clause in a prior version. Borrower's counsel checks the metadata
and requests the same change for their client's documents. The partner tells
you to go ahead and delete the clause. The partner says the bank includes it
because some borrowers don't realize it's unenforceable. Then they tell you
to hurry up and get the loan closed. You are rushing when you print out the
documents, and accidentally print out the original unchanged version for the
borrower to sign. After the closing, the senior partner catches the mistake
and tells you to correct it by attaching the signature page to the revised
version. The partner also sees you forgot to get the signature on the
mortgage notarized. But you are able to correct that mistake as well; you
take the document to a notary and have them notarize it after the fact. You
then have the loan documents compiled and bound, and send them to the

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
client with a letter explaining the substantive requirements in the
documents. You aren't in the office when the letter is typed up, so you ask
your paralegal to sign your name and send it out.

How many ethical issues do you see?

I'm sure every law student knows you can't defraud your own client or lie
to the court. Among other things, attorneys are prohibited in the ethical
rules from engaging "in conduct involving dishonesty, fraud, deceit, or
misrepresentation...." E.g., Florida Rules of Professional Conduct, Rule 4-
8.4(c). But there are a lot of subtle ways you could find yourself doing
something in connection with your drafting that could be considered
dishonest.

Is it ethical, for example, to include in a contract a provision you know is
unenforceable, like an "ipso facto" clause (entitling the lender to call the
loan if the borrower files a bankruptcy petition), for the purpose of
deceiving a party as to his rights? E.g., Florida Rules of Professional
Conduct, 4-1.2(d)("A lawyer shall not counsel a client to engage, or assist a
client, in conduct that the lawyer knows or reasonably should know is
criminal or fraudulent."); See, Alexandra Sickler, Ethics in Contract
Drafting, 64 Gavel 10.

You probably know what metadata is (information attached to a document
that is not visible on the face of the document); but are you may not be in
the habit of scrubbing the metadata from your word documents before you
provide them to the client or a third party? What if you circulate a set of
loan documents you drafted and accidentally include in the metadata
confidential information about the client? It is clear an attorney has an
ethical obligation to keep client information confidential. E.g., Florida
Rules of Professional Conduct, 4-1.6(a). Are you in violation of the rule if
you accidentally reveal that confidential information to a third party? See
e.g., Florida Rules of Professional Conduct, 4-1.6(e) ("A lawyer must make
reasonable efforts to prevent the inadvertent or unauthorized disclosure of,
or unauthorized access to, information relating to the representation of a
client").

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
What if you fail to keep track of the final version of a document and
accidentally arrange for the signing of an early draft at the closing? You are
implicitly representing the document is the final version by producing it at
the closing, but the misrepresentation is not intentional. Again, does it
matter that you did it by accident? See e.g., Florida Rules of Professional
Conduct, Rule 4-1.1 (Attorneys must also "provide competent
representation," which requires "the legal knowledge, skill, thoroughness,
and preparation reasonably necessary for the representation.")

I think it's a clear ethical violation to have a client sign a blank signature
page, and then substitute the document later. If the client signed a blank
page and then you made it look like the client signed the document you
attached, that is clearly deceitful. But what if you substitute one document
for a revised version after the document is signed? Does it matter whether
the client knows about the substitution, and consents to the new document's
terms, or whether the changes are material, or only affect the rights of
another party to the document? The client still didn't do what it has been
made to look like the client did, right?

What if a senior attorney tells you to do it? Is that an excuse? "A lawyer is
bound by the Rules of Professional Conduct notwithstanding that the
lawyer acted at the direction of another person." E.g., Florida Rules of
Professional Conduct, Rule 4-5.2(a). So, what would you do? Would you
refuse? Or is there some better way of handling the situation?

It isn't unusual for parties to separately sign duplicate originals of a contract
if they are in two different places when the closing occurs. And there is no
problem with the closing attorney subsequently combining the signature
pages to form one fully signed document. What is the difference between
that situation and the one in the hypothetical?

If a client has already signed a document, can the attorney have it notarized
after the fact? The notary's jurat usually states that the signer "subscribed
before me." Subscribed means signed, so isn't the notary attesting to a
misrepresentation if the signer didn't actually sign in front of the notary?

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
And finally, is it o.k. to have your paralegal sign your name to a cover
letter? If the letter just says, "Enclosed please find copies of the
documents," that's one thing. But the paralegal's identity and nonlawyer
status should still be disclosed. If the letter discusses the substantive
content of the documents, that's likely different. Among other things, a
lawyer may be aiding in the unauthorized practice of law by allowing a
nonlawyer to sign a letter containing legal advice. See, Florida Rules of
Professional Conduct, 4-5.5(b)(2) (A nonlawyer may not "hold out to the
public or otherwise represent that the lawyer is admitted to practice in
Florida.").

Even more important than the issue of whether common occurrences like
these constitute ethical violations is the issue of how to handle them should
they arise. You may have noticed most of these issues are the result of
someone making a mistake and then looking for a quick, easy way to fix it.
Mistakes happen. Everyone makes mistakes from time to time. That isn't
the problem. The problem is how you handle the mistakes when they occur.
To avoid violating the rules of ethics, I would recommend that you follow
these three steps.

First, figure out how to properly correct whatever went wrong. Probably
you are going have to re-do whatever you did. If the problem happened in
connection with a closing, that usually means having the document signed
again.

Second, be up front about what happened. Don't try to cover it up. When
you tell the affected party what happened also tell them how you are going
to fix it. Don't just "drop a bomb" on someone's lap and then leave them to
figure out what to do.

Third, correct the mistake on your own time and at your own expense (don't
bill the client for the time you spend correcting your own error). And fix
the problem a way that is as little of an inconvenience as possible to your
client and/or your employer. So if you need a page initialed, bring the
document to the client's place of business; don't ask them to take time off
from work and come into your office to get that done.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
I remember my ethics professor in law school saying "Your license is your
ticket to practice law. Whatever you do, don't do anything that could put
your ticket in jeopardy." Unfortunately, when you are really busy and under
a lot of stress, there are shortcuts you might be tempted to take that might
seem perfectly innocent at the time. But later, with 20/20 hindsight, what
you did might also be characterized as inappropriate. Despite the rush of
activity, you still have to pay close attention and stay on top of what you are
doing to avoid ending up on the wrong side of an ethics complaint.

                                  Practice Exercises
         Scrub the metadata from the Associate Employment Agreement,
 Laptop Purchase Agreement, Residential Lease Agreement, Sale of
 Assets Agreement and/or Consulting Services Agreement you drafted as
 the practice exercises for the previous chapters.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
13. GETTING THE CONTRACT
SIGNED (CLOSING THE
DEAL)

I 've been hired to draft a lot of documents, but I don't think there has
      ever been a time when my job wasn't also to get the documents signed
      and the deal done. Sometimes you just need a signature, but more
often there is money to collect as well. If the signer is signing for an entity,
you should always get some verification of the signer's authority. If the
deal is complex enough, that will be just part of the closing agenda and due
diligence process that precedes the actual signing of the documents. And if
all goes well, don't forget to follow-up with the client and make sure the
door is open for them to refer more work your way.

A. Get the Documents Signed and Collect the Money

The last step in drafting, just like the first step (getting up to speed) has
little to do with drafting itself. It is diligently following-up and getting the
deal closed. You would be surprised how many times a project "falls
through the cracks," especially when you are busy, and your attention is
called to other bigger and better things.

And it isn't just the signing you have to get done. If the agreement involves
a deposit that is supposed to be collected at closing, your job is to get the
money and make sure it goes where it is supposed to go. If you don't do
that, there could be hell to pay. If there is a breach by one of the parties

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
later on, and the other party's remedy is to keep the deposit, that party isn't
going to be happy to find out you never got it.

B. Verify Authority to Sign for an Entity

If one of the persons signing the documents is signing on behalf of an
entity, there are a number of things you need to confirm to make sure the
signature will be binding on the entity. Make sure the person is who he
says he is by asking to see their driver's license. And consider having them
swear or affirm that they are signing voluntarily by having their signature
notarized.

In addition, review the state statutes for the type of entity involved to
confirm that the entity has the power to enter into the agreement, and to
determine who has the authority to enter into the agreement on behalf of the
entity. For example, in Florida, the powers of a corporation are enumerated
in § 607.0392, Fla. Stat.; and the authority to exercise those powers is given
to the directors of the corporation in § 607.0801, Fla. Stat.

Then look at the entity's organizational documents (e.g., for a corporation,
the articles and bylaws) to analyze the same issues (i.e. make sure the
transaction is not "ultra vires" or beyond the corporation's powers and the
signer has been properly authorized by the directors). Usually, the purposes
of the corporation are set forth at the beginning of the articles of
organization; and power of the directors to manage the corporation is set
forth in the bylaws. Also, the bylaws will typically state that third parties
may rely on a certificate of the clerk as to the authority of someone else to
enter into the transaction and sign the documents, like this:

 5.5 Evidence of Authority. A certificate by the Clerk or Secretary, as to
 any action taken by the stockholders, Directors, any committee or any
 officer or representative of the corporation shall as to all persons who rely
 on the certificate in good faith be conclusive evidence of such action.

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
Relying on a provision like that in the bylaws, you would require the entity
entering into the agreement to produce a clerk's certificate and resolutions
evidencing the signer's authority. The clerk's certificate identifies the signer
and the signer's title, and authenticates the attached resolutions which
confirm the signer is authorized to sign on behalf of the entity. Here is an
example for a corporation:

            SECRETARY'S CERTIFICATE

The undersigned, on behalf of Cigars for Everyone, Inc., a Florida
corporation (the "Corporation") and not individually, certifies that:

1. Attached to this certificate as Exhibit A is a true, correct and
complete copy of the Action of Directors at a Meeting of the Directors
of the Corporation, duly adopted by the Directors on April 12, 2020,
which Action has not been revoked, modified, amended, or rescinded
and is in full force and effect as of the date of this certificate (the
"Action").

2. The Corporation is a non-profit corporation legally existing and
in good standing under the laws of the State of Florida, as of the date
of this certificate, and has the power, authority and legal right to
enter into, and to execute, deliver, and perform, the obligations under
the documents listed in the Action.

3. The following persons have been duly elected and qualified, and
on the date hereof are officers of the Corporation, holding the
respective offices set opposite their names:

 Name          Title

Sam Zapata     President

Eileen Zapata  Secretary

       Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
The undersigned, has, on behalf of the Corporation, executed this
certificate on this 22nd day of April, 2020.

       ______________________________
       Eileen Zapata, Secretary

                                      Exhibit A

        ACTIONS OF DIRECTORS BY WRITTEN CONSENT

                      CIGARS FOR EVERYONE, INC.

MEETING: April 12, 2020

Pursuant to the Articles of Organization and By-Laws of Cigars for
Everyone, Inc., a Florida corporation (the "Corporation"), the
Directors of the Corporation do adopt the following resolutions and
agree to the taking of the actions set forth below:

RESOLVED: That the Corporation is empowered, authorized,
and directed to take all action and execute and deliver any documents
necessary or proper to direct, complete, carry out, consummate and
effectuate the transfer, conveyance, or sale of the project located on
the property at 101 University Avenue, Gainesville, Florida (the
"Property").

RESOLVED: That the President of the Corporation, Sam
Zapata, be, and is, empowered, authorized, and directed in the name
of and on behalf of the Corporation to take all action and execute and
deliver any documents necessary or proper to direct, complete, carry
out, consummate, and effectuate the transfer, conveyance or sale of
the Property to the University of Florida, including, without
limitation, a quitclaim deed of the Property from the Corporation to
the University of Florida, a Settlement Statement, 1099 Form and
Property Owner's Affidavit.

                         Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
If the transaction involves a loan in a substantial amount it may also be
appropriate to request and opinion from borrower's counsel that the signer is
authorized, and the loan documents are enforceable against the entity. If
there is a mortgage involved, it may be appropriate to go a step further and
request insurance from a title company that the mortgage creates and
enforceable lien on the property, and the lien has the required priority over
other liens. The issue of whether a signer for an entity is properly
authorized is central to any closing, but for very large and complex deals, it
is also just the tip of the iceberg.

C. Create a Closing Agenda

If the deal is something like a commercial loan for the construction of a
multi-unit building, then the process will likely involve the creation of a
closing agenda or checklist, which is a list of due diligence items that need
to be produced, reviewed, and approved before the documents are actually
signed and the deal closed. Here is an example of a short closing checklist:

Closing Checklist

A $1,000,000.00 Mortgage loan from Fictitious Bank (Lender) to
Borrower Corporation (Borrower). Items marked B are to be
submitted by the Borrower. Items marked L are the responsibility of
Lender.

A. Organization and Authority of Borrower

1. Articles of Organization, and amendments  B

2. Bylaws, and any amendments                                    B

Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
3. Current Legal Existence / Good Standing        B
    Certificate                                            B

4. Evidence of Authority for Transactions
    (Clerk's Certificate)

B. Loan Documents

1. $1,000,000.00 Promissory Note                                 L

2. Mortgage and Security Agreement                               L

3. Loan Agreement with all exhibits attached      L

4. UCC Financing Statements                                         L

C. Title Insurance Matters

1. Copy of Title Insurance Commitment

provided to Lender                                                     B

2. Title Exception Documents                                        B

D. Legal Opinions

1. Opinion of Borrower's Counsel as to

execution, delivery, validity and enforceability

of Loan Documents                                                      B

2. Opinion of Borrower's Counsel as to compliance with building,

zoning, and subdivision laws

and regulations                                                         B

E. Other Documents

1. Copy of Building Permit                                           B
2. Environmental Site Assessment                                 B
3. Project Development Budget
4. Contract with General Contractor                                B
                                                                  B

Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
 5. Subcontracts

The agenda also helps to keep everyone focused and on track in a large
commercial project involving a lot of attorneys. Using e-mail helps keep
people in the loop too. At the beginning of every closing I handled, one of
the first things I did was get everyone's e-mail address. E-mail is an
effective way to keep everyone in the loop and deliver copies of documents,
drafts, and other closing materials.
I used to also participate in periodic conference calls for the closings I was
involved in. If I were doing closings today, I'd do the conferences online
with a service like Zoom, Uberconference or Skype. The advantage of an
online conference is that you can put a document on the screen during the
conference so everyone else can see it. You can revise the document in real
time as various modifications are discussed. And you can keep a recording
of what was said and done by the parties and their attorneys while the
conference took place, assuming everyone consents to being recorded.

D. Follow Up After the Closing

                          Electronic copy available at: https://ssrn.com/abstract=4294001
After the deal is done, it is usually counsel's responsibility to distribute to
the parties either the original, signed documents or copies of the signed
documents. In the old days, attorneys would distribute a closing "bible"
containing all the relevant documents. Today you will most likely send out
a CD containing pdfs of the same documents. When you send that package
out to the client, I would also recommend sending the client a cover letter
highlighting any deadlines or other important provisions in the agreement
the client will need to remember. Put together a nice organized package so
the client can see what they paid for and thank them for the opportunity to
be of service. The end of every transaction, assuming all went well, is a
good time to continue developing your relationship with the client.

Law is a service industry. You can't save the world if the world does not
want to hire you to do the work. In the same way that you should always
look for opportunities to add value to a deal, you should also be on the
lookout for opportunities to further develop your relationships with clients.
As long as you have clients, you will never have trouble finding a job.

 drafted as the practice exercises for the previous chapters.

(Frank Rodriguez, Treasurer, will be signing for AutoDocs Consulting
